The Mu'tazilite Argument Against the Subsistence of Motion
Argument against motion subsistence with objection and reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that motion cannot subsist because it would become rest, and the subtle distinction between motion from and motion to a place that allows for a different view.

Core Idea
Motion cannot subsist because persistent motion would be identical to rest.

This lesson explores a key intra-Mu'tazilite debate about the nature of motion. After agreeing that accidents subsist, the Mu'tazilites split on whether motion endures. The argument presented here—that if motion persisted, it would become rest—is countered by a distinction between motion *from* a place and motion *to* a place. Understanding this debate sharpens your grasp of how theologians reason about physical reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: agreement on accidents, different opinions on motion, entailment, falsity, objection, and answer.
The passage is dense; go slowly.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the core claim: if motion subsists, it becomes rest. Then examine the entailment and why the consequent is false.
Key terms: بقاء (subsistence), حركة (motion), سكون (rest), كون (accident).
3
Understand the objection and reply
The objection distinguishes motion and rest by their effects. The reply denies that motion necessitates departure and distinguishes motion *from* vs. *to* a place.
This distinction is crucial for the answer.
4
Trace Abu Hashim's position
Notice how Abu Hashim's view (motion subsists and is identical to rest in the second moment) resolves the argument by identifying the two accidents as one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها أنهم بعد اتفاقهم على بقاء الأعراض اختلفوا في بقاء الحركة فنفاه الجبائي وأكثر المعتزلة إذا لو بقيت الحركة كانت سكوناً والتالي باطل أما الملازمة فإذاً لا معنى للسكون إلا الكون المستمر في حيز واحد والحركة هي الكون في الحيز الأول فلو كانت باقية كانت في الزمن الثاني كوناً مستمراً في الحيز الثاني فيكون عين السكون وأما بطلان التالي فلتضاد الحركة والسكون ومن المستحيل أن يكون أحد الضدين عين الآخر وبالجملة فالحاصل في الآن الثاني في الحيز الثاني سكون بالاتفاق فيجب أن يكون الحاصل في الآن الثاني كونا آخر متجدداً لا الكون الأول الذي هو حركة وإلا فالسكون هو الحركة بعينه والضرورة تنفيه كيف والحركة التي هي الكون الأول في الحيز الثاني توجب الخروج عن ذلك الحيز دون السكون الذي ذكرناه وهو الكون الثاني في الحيز الثاني فإنه لا يوجب ذلك الخروج فيتغايران قطعاً ويمكن الجواب بمنع بطلان التالي بما مر من أن المنافي للسكون والمضاد له هو الحركة من الحيز فإنها لا تجامع السكون فيه لا الحركة إليه فإنها لا تنافي السكون فيه فجاز أن تكون الحركة إلى مكان عين السكون فيه وقولهم الحركة توجب الخروج عن الحيز الأول ليس بصحيح لأنها لا توجب الخروج عنه بل الحركة هو الخروج عن الحيز الأول وأنه نفس الحصول في الحيز الثاني الذي هو السكون فإن قلت لا يخفى أن الكون الأول في الحيز الثاني هو عين الخروج عن الحيز الأول كما ذكرتم إلا الكون الثاني في الحيز الثاني ليس عين الخروج عن الأول فهما متغايران قلت إنما يصح ذلك إن لو ثبت تعدد الكونين في الحيز الثاني على تقدير اتحادهما كان الثاني منهما كالأول عين الخروج عن الحيز الأول وبه قال أبو هاشم أي إنه قال ببقاء الحركة وبأن الكون الأول في الحيز الثاني هو الحركة وهو بعينه الكون الذي في الزمن الثاني المسمى بالسكون
One of them is that after their agreement on the subsistence of accidents, they differed on the subsistence of motion: al-Jubba'i and most Mu'tazilites denied it, because if motion subsisted it would be rest, and the consequent is false. As for the entailment: there is no meaning of rest except a continuous accident (*kawn*) in one place, and motion is the accident in the first place. So if it were subsisting, it would be in the second moment a continuous accident in the second place, and thus it would be identical to rest. As for the falsity of the consequent: because of the opposition between motion and rest, and it is impossible for one opposite to be identical to the other. In summary, what occurs in the second moment in the second place is rest by agreement, so it must be another new accident, not the first accident which is motion. Otherwise rest would be identical to motion, and necessity rejects that. How? For motion, which is the first accident in the second place, necessitates departure from that place, unlike rest which we mentioned—the second accident in the second place—since it does not necessitate that departure, so they are certainly different. And an answer is possible by denying the falsity of the consequent based on what was mentioned earlier: that what is incompatible with rest and opposite to it is motion *from* a place, for it does not coexist with rest in it, not motion *to* it, for it does not conflict with rest in it. So it is possible for motion to a place to be identical to rest in it. And their statement that motion necessitates departure from the first place is not correct, because it does not necessitate departure from it; rather motion is the departure from the first place and it is the very occurrence in the second place which is rest. If you say: It is obvious that the first accident in the second place is identical to departure from the first place as you mentioned, but the second accident in the second place is not identical to departure from the first, so they are different. I say: That would be valid only if the multiplicity of the two accidents in the second place were established; but on the assumption of their unity, the second of them would be like the first, identical to departure from the first place. And this is what Abu Hashim said, i.e., he affirmed the subsistence of motion and that the first accident in the second place is motion, and it is identical to the accident in the second moment called rest.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents a Mu'tazilite argument against the subsistence of motion, followed by an objection and a reply. It begins by stating that after agreeing that accidents subsist, the Mu'tazilites differed about motion. Al-Jubba'i and most of them denied its subsistence. Their proof: if motion were to remain, it would be rest. The entailment is that 'rest' means a continuous accident in one place, while 'motion' is an accident in the first place; if motion persisted into the second moment, it would be a continuous accident in the second place, which is exactly rest. The falsity of the consequent is due to the opposition between motion and rest—one opposite cannot be the other. The conclusion: the accident in the second moment must be a new accident, not the original motion. Then an objection is raised: motion necessitates departure from the first place, rest does not, so they differ. The reply distinguishes between motion *from* a place and motion *to* a place: only motion *from* a place is incompatible with rest in that place, while motion *to* a place is not, so motion to a place could be identical to rest in it. Furthermore, motion is not the cause of departure but is itself the departure and the occurrence in the second place. A further objection is met by noting that if the two accidents are truly one, then the single accident is both motion and rest. Abu Hashim held this view, affirming that motion subsists and that the first accident in the second place is motion, which is the same as the accident called rest.

حركة
سكون
كون
الجبائي
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion and rest are always absolutely incompatible and cannot be identical in any sense.
Correction: The passage shows that motion *to* a place does not conflict with rest in that place, and on the view of Abu Hashim, motion subsisting is identical to rest.

Checkpoint

A) Because motion cannot subsist at all.
B) Because the accident in the second moment is rest by agreement, and motion cannot be rest.
C) Because al-Jubba'i denied subsistence of motion.
D) Because motion necessitates departure from the first place.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite debate on motion ends with a subtle distinction: motion *to* a place can be rest, enabling Abu Hashim's view that motion subsists.

Al-Jubba'i's Exceptions to the Subsistence of Rest
Al-Jubba'i's two exceptions to the subsistence of rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two exceptions al-Jubba'i made to the general Mu'tazilite doctrine of the subsistence of rest and the reasoning behind each exception.

Core Idea
Rest subsists generally, but in two specific cases it must be renewed.

This lesson explores a nuanced disagreement within the Mu'tazilite school about the nature of rest. While most Mu'tazilites, following Abu Hashim, held that rest subsists over time, al-Jubba'i identified two exceptions where rest must be renewed. Understanding these exceptions reveals key principles of their atomistic physics and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Then read the translation to ensure you understand the details.
Pay attention to the two 'exceptions' (صورتين) that al-Jubba'i makes.
2
Identify the two exceptions
After reading, list the two exceptions al-Jubba'i makes to the general rule that rest subsists. Note the specific conditions for each.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each exception
For each exception, ask: why did al-Jubba'i require rest to be renewed rather than subsisting? The passage gives a reason for each.
The first exception uses the principle that the newly occurring is stronger than the subsisting.
4
Reflect on the broader theological implications
Consider how al-Jubba'i's exceptions fit into his overall theological system, especially regarding divine power (قدرة) and human action.
The second exception connects to the Mu'tazilite view that power only produces new acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيها أنه ذهب أبو هاشم وأكثر المعتزلة إلى بقاء السكون من غير تفصيل واستثنى الجبائي ومن تابعه صورتين الأولى ما إذا هوى جسم ثقيل بما فيه من الاعتمادات المتجددة فأمسكه الله تعالى من غير أن يكون تحته ما يقله فلا بد من تجدد السكون فيه وإنما ذهب إلى ذلك لأن من أصله أن الطارئ الحادث أقوى من الباقي فلو كان السكون باقياً لا متجدداً لهوى ذلك الجسم الثقيل بما يتجدد فيه من الاعتمادات الصورة الثانية السكون المقدور للحي فإنه لا بد أن يكون متجدداً إذ لو بقي لم يكن مقدوراً لأن تأثير القدرة إنما هو الأحداث ولا الأحداث حالة البقاء فيجب حينئذ لو أمر الحي بالحركة ولم يتحرك
The second of them is that Abu Hashim and most Mu'tazilites held the subsistence of rest without qualification, but al-Jubba'i and those who followed him excepted two cases: the first is when a heavy body falls due to what is in it of renewed inclinations (i'timadāt) and God holds it without there being beneath it something to support it, then the rest in it must be renewed. He held this because his principle is that the newly occurring (tāri') is stronger than the subsisting, so if the rest were subsisting not renewed, that heavy body would fall due to the inclinations renewed in it. The second case is the rest that is subject to the power (maqdūr) of a living being, for it must be renewed, because if it subsisted it would not be subject to power, since the effect of power is only to bring about (ījād), and there is no bringing about in a state of subsistence. Hence, if the living being were commanded to move and did not move, [it would be impossible].
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite disagreement: the subsistence of rest. Abu Hashim and most Mu'tazilites affirm that rest subsists generally, but al-Jubba'i and his followers make two exceptions. The first exception: when a heavy body falls due to its own renewed inclinations (i'timadāt) and God holds it in place without any physical support, the rest in that body must be renewed moment by moment. The reasoning is al-Jubba'i's principle that a newly occurring attribute is stronger than a subsisting one; if the rest were subsisting, it would be weaker than the renewed inclinations and the body would fall. The second exception: the rest of a living being that is subject to its power (maqdūr). This rest cannot subsist because power only produces new acts; it cannot affect something already subsisting. Therefore, the rest must be renewed. The implication is that if a living being were commanded to move but did not, its rest would have to be a new act of power.

Key Terms
بقاء السكون
الجبائي
أبو هاشم
اعتماد
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That rest always subsists in all cases without exception.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i shows that in two specific cases—when a heavy body is held by God without support and when rest is subject to a living being's power—rest must be renewed, not subsisting.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Jubba'i except the case of a heavy body held by God without support from the subsistence of rest?
A) Because God's holding prevents any rest.
B) Because the inclinations are stronger than subsisting rest, so rest must be renewed.
C) Because heavy bodies cannot have rest at all.
D) Because Abu Hashim disagreed.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's exceptions demonstrate that the subsistence of rest is not absolute; it is limited by physical and volitional principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that even your rest is not self-subsisting but must be continuously renewed by God's power, how does that change your sense of dependence on Him in every moment? Are you aware of this constant renewal, or do you take your existence for granted?
?
Al-Jubba'i argued that a heavy body would fall if its rest were merely subsisting. What 'heavy' burdens in your life are you carrying as if they are static, forgetting that only God's continuous holding keeps them in place? How can you cultivate a moment-by-moment trust in His preservation?
?
If your power to act only brings about new acts and never sustains a state, then every moment you are given a fresh ability to move or rest. How does this insight affect your view of your own decisions? Are you using each new moment of power as a gift, or are you acting as if it is automatically yours?

The Second Scenario of Rest and the Nickname 'al-Dhihni'
Explains the second scenario of persistent rest and why Abu Hashim was called al-dhihni

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili debate on persistent and renewed rest regarding sinfulness, and why Abu Hashim was nicknamed 'al-dhihni'.

Core Idea
Persistent rest is not within human power; sin requires an act within power.

This passage delves into a specific Mu'tazili scenario concerning the rulings of rest and motion. It explains the second scenario of rest and the nickname 'al-dhihni' given to Abu Hashim. We will explore the theological reasoning behind sin and punishment in this context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of parentheses and the structure of the argument.
2
Identify the two scenarios of rest
The passage distinguishes between persistent rest (sukun baqi) and renewed rest (sukun mutajaddid). Understand how each is treated differently in terms of capability and sin.
Persistent rest is not within power because it is eternal; renewed rest is within power.
3
Understand Abu Hashim's position and the nickname
Abu Hashim committed to sinfulness and punishment for non-action in the scenario of renewed rest, even though the person lacks power over the opposite motion. This led to his nickname 'al-dhihni'. The passage gives two possible reasons for this nickname.
The nickname implies a mental or intellectual stance that departs from the standard Mu'tazili principle.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this debate fits into the larger Mu'tazili framework on the rulings of actions and the scope of human capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل استمر على ما كان عليه من السكون ) أن لا يأثم إذ لا إثم على أصلهم إلا على أمر مقدور، والسكون المضاد للحركة إذا كان باقياً لم يكن مقدوراً، فلا يكون آثماً به خلاف الإجماع) بخلاف ما إذا كان السكون متجدداً ( ولزم هذا) الذي ذكره ) وهو الجبائي في إثبات الصورة الثانية ( بأبي هاشم فلم يجد عنه محيصاً (والتزم) التأثم و ( العقاب بعدم الفعل في هذه الصورة مع انتفاء القدرة على ضده المستلزم لعدمه، إذ ليس هناك شيء يتصور صدوره عنه سوى هذا الضد الذي هو السكون (فلقب بالذهني ) إما لأنه رجع عن مقتضى أصولهم في أن الثواب والعقاب، إنما يتعلقان بما يصدر عن المكلف بقدرته وستر مذهبه في الذهن وإما لأنه أثبت التأثم والعقاب أمر يدرك بالذهن، وليس صادراً عن المكلف أصلاً
قوله : ( والسكون المضاد للحركة وكذا عدم الحركة لأنه أزلي لا يتعلق به القدرة . قوله : (التأثم والعقاب بعدم (الفعل) أي بعدم الحركة في هذه الصورة المخصوصة مع انتفاء القدرة على ضده المستلزم لعدم الحركة أعني السكون . قوله : (إذ ليس هناك إلخ) تعليل لانتفاء القدرة على الضد أي لا يتصور منه هناك أي فيما إذا لم يتحرك إلا صدور السكون وقد فرض أنه غير مقدور لأنه باق والشيء حالة البقاء غير مقدور . قوله : (إما لأنه رجع إلخ ) لأنه التزم العقاب بعدم الحركة مع أنه يلزم أن يقول بإحساس جميع المعاني الجزئية التي يدرك العقل التفرقة بينها بواسطة الإحساس كالحسن والقبح والعداوة والصداقة والفرح والحزن وليس كذلك فإنها معقولة عندنا في الحواس الباطنة موهومة عند مثبتيها .
Rather, he continued upon what he was upon of rest) that he is not sinful, for there is no sin according to their principle except upon an act that is within power, and rest opposite to motion, if it is persistent, is not within power, so he is not sinful by it, contrary to consensus) unlike if the rest is renewed (and this) which he mentioned) namely al-Jubba'i in establishing the second scenario (was incumbent upon Abu Hashim, and he found no way out) and he committed himself to sinfulness and punishment for non-action in this scenario, despite the absence of power over its opposite necessitating its non-existence, since there is nothing conceivable as proceeding from him except this opposite which is rest (so he was nicknamed al-dhihni) either because he reverted from the implication of their principles that reward and punishment are attached only to what proceeds from the obligated person by his power, and he concealed his doctrine in the mind, or because he affirmed sinfulness and punishment as something perceived by the mind, not proceeding from the obligated person at all. His saying: (And the rest opposite to motion and likewise the absence of motion because it is eternal, power does not attach to it. His saying: (Sinfulness and punishment for non-action) i.e., for non-motion in this specific scenario, with the absence of power over its opposite necessitating the non-existence of motion, namely rest. His saying: (Since there is not there, etc.) is a reason for the absence of power over the opposite, i.e., nothing is conceivable from him there, i.e., in the case where he does not move, except the occurrence of rest, and it has been assumed that it is not within power because it is persistent, and a thing in the state of persistence is not within power. His saying: (Either because he reverted, etc.) because he committed to punishment for non-motion, although it necessitates that he should affirm the sensing of all particular meanings that the mind distinguishes between them by means of sensation, like good and evil, enmity and friendship, joy and sorrow, and it is not so, for they are intelligible to us in the inner senses, imaginary according to those who affirm them.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific scenario within the Mu'tazili debate on the rulings of accidents (akwan). It begins by explaining the second scenario concerning rest. If a person remains in a state of rest (sukun) that is persistent (baqi), according to Mu'tazili principles, he should not be deemed sinful for failing to move because the persistent rest is not within his power; sin only attaches to acts within one's capability. However, if the rest is renewed (mutajaddid), a different ruling applies. Al-Jubba'i established this second scenario, and his son Abu Hashim found no escape from it. Abu Hashim committed to the view that the person is sinful and deserves punishment for not performing the act (i.e., not moving) in this scenario, even though he lacks power over the opposite (motion), since nothing else can proceed from him except that rest. For this, Abu Hashim was nicknamed 'al-dhihni' (the mentalist). Two reasons are given: either because he departed from the Mu'tazili principle that reward and punishment attach only to what the obligated person does by his power, and he concealed his true doctrine in his mind; or because he affirmed sinfulness and punishment for something perceived only by the mind, not actually proceeding from the obligated person. The subsequent glosses explain the meaning of 'rest opposite to motion' and the absence of power over persistent states, and further clarify that Abu Hashim's position implies a problematic extension of sensation to abstract meanings like good and evil, which his opponents deny as being merely intelligible, not sensed.

الحركة
القدرة
الذهني
التأثم
العقاب
أبو هاشم
الجبائي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that persistent rest is within human power and thus its continuation is punishable.
Correction: The passage clarifies that persistent rest is not within power because it is an eternal state; only renewed rest can be subject to moral responsibility.

Checkpoint
Why was Abu Hashim nicknamed 'al-dhihni' according to the passage?
A) Because he affirmed that motion and rest are perceived by the senses.
B) Because he held that sinfulness and punishment can attach to something not proceeding from the obligated person's power, perceived only by the mind.
C) Because he rejected the Mu'tazili principle that reward and punishment depend on acts within power.
D) Because he argued that rest is always within human power.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's nickname 'al-dhihni' arose from his commitment to sinful punishment for non-action in the scenario of renewed rest, despite the absence of power over the opposite, which contradicted the Mu'tazili principle that reward and punishment depend on acts within power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you sometimes feel guilty for things you could not have changed? How does this lesson's principle—that sin requires an act within your power—challenge those feelings? Will you consciously release that false guilt today?
?
Abu Hashim followed his reasoning even when it made him stand out and earned him a nickname. Is there a truth you have been avoiding because it would make you uncomfortable or unpopular? What cost are you willing to pay to follow evidence where it leads?
?
This passage shows that even among scholars, holding to a principle (like power for sin) required careful thinking. Are you quick to judge yourself or others without considering whether the act was truly within their power? How can you apply this lesson's nuance to your relationships?

Motion and Rest Are Not Perceptible: The Mu'tazilite Argument
Al-Jurjani argues that motion is not distinctively perceptible, with examples from a ship and sleep.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that motion and rest are not perceptible by the senses, based on the principle that perception requires particularity and that the particularities of spatial states are indistinguishable.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are not perceived; only the particularities of spatial states, which are imperceptible, exist.

This passage from al-Jurjani presents a central proof in the debate over the perceptibility of motion and rest. The Mu'tazilites argued that motion and rest are merely states (akwān) in locations, and since these states lack sensorily distinguishable particularities, they cannot be perceived by the senses. Through vivid examples of a ship passenger and a person moved in sleep, the argument illustrates that we do not directly perceive motion or rest themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the overall argument. Notice the dense structure with embedded commentary.
The passage includes both the main argument and the commentator's clarifications.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position on kawn
Note that motion and rest are considered the same type of state (kawn) in different moments. The argument equates motion with kawn.
This is a key premise for the proof.
3
Examine the principle of perception
Perception requires a particularity (khuṣūṣiyya); absolute existence is not perceived. Since kawn in a location has no sensorily distinguishable particularity, it is imperceptible.
This principle is crucial for the proof.
4
Analyze the ship example
The passenger cannot distinguish the particularities of the ship's states in different airy locations, so motion is not perceived. This shows the imperceptibility of kawn.
5
Analyze the sleep example
A person moved in sleep to a different location senses no change upon waking, confirming that the particularities of states are not perceived.
This reinforces the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج ( بأن ) الحركة عين الكون في الحيز بعد أن كان في غيره وذلك الكون هو السكون بعينه في الزمن الثاني كما هو مذهبه ثم إن ( الكون ) ليس مدركاً بالحواس إذ لو كان مدركا لكان مدركا بخصوصيته إذ الإدراك عندهم لا يتعلق بمطلق الوجود بل بخصوصية المدرك واللازم باطل فإن خصوصية الكون في الأحياز المعينة غير يرى أن ( راكب السفينة قد لا يدرك حركة السفينة ولا سكون الشط ) فإنها إذا كانت سهلة الجري على الماء غير مضطربة عليه فإن راكبها لا يدرك تفرقة بين خصوصيات أكوانها في الأحياز الهوائية المتبدلة عليها بخرقها الهواء بل ربما توهم أنها ساكنة في حيز واحد من الهواء وأن الشط متحرك إلى خلاف جهة حركتها ( ومن نقل في النوم إِلى غير حيزه تبدل عليه كونه بكون آخر ( فإذا استيقظ لم يدركه ولم يجد اختلافاً في حالتيه مع القطع باختلاف الكونين المخصصين له بالحيزين، ويظهر ذلك فيمن كان هاوياً فى الجو متبدلاً أحيازه عليه فلو غلبه عيناه وهو في حيز وانتقل منه في قومه إلى حيز آخر ثم استيقظ فإنه لا يجد تفرقة بين كونيه في حيزيه (بخلاف ما لو (لون في نومه (بغير لونه) فإنه يدركه ويميزه عن لونه السابق بالضرورة ) قوله : ( وذلك الكون إلخ زاد هذا الكلام على المتن لئلا يرد أن الاحتجاج المذكور إنما يدل على أن الحركة ليست مدركة بحاسة البصر والمدعى أن الحركة والسكون كليهما ليسا بمدركين بهما عندهم خلافاً للأشاعرة فإن الرؤية عندهم بمطلق الوجود قوله : (بمطلق الوجود الصواب أن يقال بمطلق الكون في الحيز إذ الكلام فيه لا الوجود . وهي أن قوله : ( لا يدرك تفرقة بين خصوصيات (أكوانها أشار بذلك إلى دفع مناقشة التنوير المذكور مصادرة حيث نور عدم إدراك خصوصية الكون بالبصر بعدم إدراك حركة السفينة وسكون الشط الذي هو المدعى، ووجه الدفع أنه أراد بعدم إدراك خصوصيات أكوانها . قوله : ( بل ربما توهم إلخ) أي تحكم باطل بخلاف ما في نفس الأمر فلا يكون التفرق بين خصوصيات الأكوان مدركة .
He argued that motion is the same as the state (kawn) in the spatial location after it was in another, and that state is the very same as rest in the second moment as per his doctrine. Then, the state is not perceptible by the senses, for if it were perceptible, it would be perceived by its particularity, since perception according to them does not attach to absolute existence but to the particularity of the perceived. The consequent is false, because the particularity of the state in specific spatial locations is not seen. Consider that a ship passenger may not perceive the motion of the ship nor the stillness of the shore; for if the ship runs smoothly on water without turbulence, its passenger does not perceive a distinction between the particularities of its states in the changing airy locations as it cuts through the air. Rather, he might imagine that it is stationary in one location in the air and that the shore moves opposite to its direction. And one who is transferred in sleep to a different location finds his state changed to another state; when he wakes, he does not perceive it and finds no difference between his two states, despite the certainty that the two states assigned to him in the two locations differ. This is evident in one falling through the air with its locations changing upon him; if sleep overcomes him in a location and he moves in his sleep to another location, then wakes, he finds no distinction between his two states in his two locations. Unlike if his color were changed in sleep to another color, for he would perceive it and distinguish it from his previous color necessarily. His saying: '(and that state etc.)' - he added this statement to the text to avoid the objection that the mentioned argument only proves that motion is not perceived by the sense of sight, while the claim is that both motion and rest are not perceived by the senses according to them, contrary to the Ash'aris, for whom vision is by absolute existence. His saying: 'by absolute existence' - it should be said: by the absolute state in the location, for the discussion is about it, not existence. And it is that his saying: 'does not perceive a distinction between the particularities of its states' - he indicated thereby the refutation of the aforementioned objection of circularity, where he argued that non-perception of the particularity of the state by sight follows from non-perception of the motion of the ship and the stillness of the shore, which is precisely the claim. The refutation is that he meant by non-perception the particularities of its states. His saying: 'rather, he might imagine etc.' - meaning a false judgment contrary to what is actually the case, so there is no perception of the distinction between the particularities of states.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a proof that motion (and rest) as states (akwān) are not perceptible by the senses. The author begins by stating the Mu'tazilī doctrine that motion and rest are the same state in different moments. The proof relies on the principle that perception requires a specific particularity; absolute existence is not perceived. Since the particularity of a state in a given location is not sensorily distinguishable—as shown by the examples of the ship passenger who cannot tell if the ship is moving or the shore is moving, and of the sleeper who is moved to a different location but upon waking senses no change—it follows that states are not perceived. The commentary clarifies that the argument targets the claim that both motion and rest are imperceptible, and that the examples are not circular because they show the lack of sensory distinction of particularities. The commentator also adjusts the phrasing from 'absolute existence' to 'absolute state in the location' to align with the topic.

حركة (ḥaraka)
سكون (sukūn)
إدراك (idrāk)
خصوصية (khuṣūṣiyya)
حيز (ḥayyiz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motion itself is directly perceived by the senses.
Correction: The passage shows that what is perceived is the particularity of the state (kawn), not the state's essence. Since the particularities of states in different locations are indistinguishable, motion and rest are not perceived.

Checkpoint

B) Because the passenger's senses are deceived by the wind
C) Because the particularity of the state (kawn) in different locations is not sensorily distinguishable
D) Because the passenger is sleeping

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites proved that motion and rest are not sensorily perceptible because perception requires distinguishable particularities, and the particularities of spatial states are indistinguishable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The ship passenger was certain the shore was moving, but your own senses can deceive you about the most basic realities. What deeply held belief about your life or faith might be based on a similar sensory illusion? Will you test it today against revelation, not just feeling?

The sleeper woke up unaware of having been moved. Are there spiritual changes in your life—shifts in your heart’s location relative to Allah—that you have not noticed because you were 'asleep'? What practice could you adopt to wake up and perceive those changes?
?
If motion and rest are not directly perceptible, then much of what you think you know about the world is inference, not direct observation. How does this challenge your reliance on physical evidence for spiritual truths? Will you commit to seeking knowledge that does not depend on sensory proof?

Fourth Argument: Composition is Perceptible by Touch and Sight
Al-Jubba'i argues composition is perceptible by touch and sight; his son objects and counters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's argument that composition is directly perceptible, and the key objections and responses in the atomist debate.

Core Idea
Composition's perceptibility is debated between direct sensing and indirect distinction.

This passage presents al-Jubba'i's fourth argument that composition (ta'līf) is tangible and visible. His son Abu Hashim objects, arguing that the distinction of shapes might be due to the states (akwān) themselves. The debate then deepens with a counter-argument and response, revealing the subtleties of atomist epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Full Passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the debate.
2
Analyze al-Jubba'i's Argument
Identify the claim that composition is perceptible and the proof from distinguishing shapes.
3
Understand the Son's Objection
Note that the son suggests the distinction may come from states (akwān) rather than composition.
4
Follow the Counter-Argument and Response
Examine Abu Hashim's contention about the unity of composition and al-Jubba'i's reply that only the upper surface's composition is perceived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعها ، قال الجبائي : التأليف ملموس ومبصر) أي مدرك بالقوة اللامسة والباصرة ( إذ ( نحن ) نفرق بين الأشكال المختلفة ) ونميز بعضها عن بعض ( وما هو إلا بالنظر إلى التأليفات المختلفة أو لمسها فلا بد أن تكون تلك التأليفات محسوسة بهاتين الحاستين ) ومنعه ابنه في أحد قوليه فقال : ذلك ) الفرق قد يكون بالنظر إلى الأكوان) أي المجاورات المختلفة المولدة للتأليفات المتفاوتة أو المحاذيات ( المتخالفة ( أوغيرها ) من الأمور المتعلقة بالجواهر سوى التأليف ) واحتج ) أبو هاشم على سبيل المعارضة ) بأن لو رؤي التأليف وهو ) أمر واحد (قائم بالصفحتين من الجسم العليا وما تحتها لرؤي الصفحتان معاً وذلك لأنَّ تأليفاً واحداً قام بكل جزأين من الصفحتين، فإذا رؤي قائماً بالصفحة العليا فقد رؤي قائماً بالصفحة التي تحتها ضرورة اتحاده ) وإنما يصح هذا الاحتجاج على أبيه ( لو لم يقل : إنَّ المدرك جواهر الصفحة العليا، وتأليف جواهرها بعضها مع بعض لا تأليف الصفحتين) يعني أنه لا يقول : إن تأليف جواهر الصفحة العليا مع ما تحتها مدرك حتى ينتهض عليه هذه المعارضة، بل يقول : إِنَّ المرئي تأليف جواهر الصفحة العليا فيما بينها . على أن لقائل أن يقول : إذا جاز عندك قيام تأليف واحد بجوهرين، فلم لا يجوز انقسامه بحيث يكون مدركاً من أحد الطرفين دون الآخر، فلا يلزم رؤية الصفحتين معا ؟ قوله : (فلم) لا يجوز إلخ) مذهبه عدم انقسام التأليف لأنه يستلزم انتفاء التفريق ولذا قال بقيام تأليف واحد بمحلين.
The fourth: al-Jubba'i said: Composition is tangible and visible, i.e., perceived by the sense of touch and sight, because we distinguish between different shapes and differentiate some from others, and that is only by looking at different compositions or touching them, so those compositions must be sensed by these two senses. His son objected in one of his sayings and said: That distinction may be by looking at the states (akwān), i.e., the different adjacencies that generate the varying compositions, or the opposing sides, or other things pertaining to the atoms except composition. And Abu Hashim argued by way of objection: If composition were seen—it being a single thing subsisting in the two surfaces of the body, the upper and what is beneath it—then both surfaces would be seen together, because a single composition subsists in each pair of parts of the two surfaces. So if it is seen subsisting in the upper surface, it would have been seen subsisting in the surface beneath it necessarily due to its unity. But this objection is valid against his father only if he did not say that what is perceived is the atoms of the upper surface and the composition of its atoms with each other, not the composition of the two surfaces. That is, he does not say that the composition of the atoms of the upper surface with what is beneath it is perceived, so that this objection would hold against him. Rather, he says that what is seen is the composition of the atoms of the upper surface among themselves. Moreover, one might say: If you allow that a single composition subsists in two atoms, why is it not possible for it to be divided such that it is perceived from one side without the other, so that seeing both surfaces together would not follow? His saying: '(so why) is it not allowed, etc.' - his doctrine is that composition is indivisible, because it would entail the negation of differentiation, and therefore he said that a single composition subsists in two loci.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument from al-Jubba'i: composition (ta'līf) is perceptible by touch and sight. The proof is that we distinguish shapes through looking at or touching compositions, so compositions must be sensible. His son (Abu Hashim) objects: the distinction might be due to the states (akwān) themselves, i.e., the adjacencies that produce compositions, not the composition directly. Then Abu Hashim presents a counter-argument: if a single composition subsists in both the upper and lower surfaces, then seeing the composition in the upper surface would entail seeing it in the lower surface, so both surfaces would be seen together. But the father (al-Jubba'i) responds that he only claims that the composition of the atoms of the upper surface among themselves is perceived, not the composition of the whole body with the lower surface. The commentary adds that the son's doctrine denies division of composition because that would negate differentiation, and he holds that a single composition can subsist in two loci.

Key Terms
تأليف (ta'līf)
ملموس (malmūs)
مبصر (mubṣar)
كون (kawn)
جوهر (jawhar)
صفحة (ṣafḥa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Composition is directly perceived as a single entity regardless of the atoms it relates to.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i argues only the composition of the upper surface is perceived, not across surfaces, and the son argues that distinction may be based on states, not composition itself.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection of Abu Hashim to his father's claim that composition is perceptible?
A) Composition cannot be perceived because it is immaterial
B) The distinction of shapes may be due to the states (akwān) rather than composition itself
C) Composition is only perceived by the sense of touch, not sight
D) The shapes themselves are not distinct

Key Takeaway
The debate over composition's perceptibility hinges on whether composition is directly sensed or inferred from states and whether a single composition can subsist in multiple loci.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you distinguish truth from falsehood in your daily life, do you rely on surface-level impressions (like al-Jubba'i's direct sensing) or do you examine the underlying 'states' (like Abu Hashim's akwān)? Which approach has led you astray before, and what did that cost you?
?
Al-Jubba'i argues that composition is directly sensed. Have you ever mistaken a superficial perception for the whole reality, only to later realize a deeper 'composition' you missed? What was that situation, and how did you correct your view?
?
The debate shows that scholars can disagree on what is perceived. When you face a conflict between what you see and what others claim, how do you ensure you are not clinging to a false perception? What would it cost you to admit you were only seeing part of the composition—and would you pay that price?

Composition and Shapes: Al-Jubba'i's Argument and Circularity
Al-Jubba'i argues composition varies with shapes; his son objects with homogeneity and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's argument that composition differs with shapes and why his son charged it with circularity.

Core Idea
Al-Jubba'i's son exposes that the argument assumes what it tries to prove.

In this passage, we encounter a classic dialectical exchange: al-Jubba'i argues that the diversity of shapes proves diversity of compositions, but his son Abu Hashim objects that compositions are essentially homogeneous. He then identifies a logical flaw: the argument's key premise already presupposes the conclusion. This lesson unpacks that charge of circular reasoning (musadara).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: claim, proof, objection, counter-objection.
2
Identify al-Jubba'i's claim and his son's two objections
Find al-Jubba'i's claim that composition differs with shapes and his proof. Then identify the son's first objection: that compositions are homogeneous. Then find the second objection: that the argument is circular.
The son's two objections are linked; he first asserts homogeneity, then shows that even if that is granted, the argument is circular.
3
Understand the charge of circularity
The son says that the premise about the most specific attribute of composition (subsisting in two loci) is only established if compositions are not different. But that is exactly what the argument tries to prove. So the premise depends on the conclusion, making the argument circular.
This is a sophisticated logical critique that shows the argument fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامسها : قال الجبائي : التأليف مختلف باختلاف الأشكال لما مر) من أنا نفرق بين الأشكال المختلفة وما هو إلا بالنظر إلى التأليفات المختلفة، فإنه لو قدر التساوي والتشابه في تأليفات الأجسام لما اختلفت أشكالها ( ومنعه ابنه ) وقال : إنَّ التأليفات متجانسة لأنَّ التأليفين مشتركان في أخص صفة النفس، وهو القيام بمحلين بناء على أصله الفاسد ( وإن سلم ) ذلك الأصل (ففيه) أي في هذا الاستدلال ) مصادرة ) لأنه يجوز أن تكون التأليفات مختلفة ومشتركة في عارض يلزمها، كون ما ذكره من أخص صفات التأليف، إنما يثبت إذا لم تكن التأليفات مختلفة فالمقدمة المذكورة في الدليل متوقفة على ثبوت المطلوب وهو المصادرة .
The fifth: al-Jubba'i said: Composition differs with differences of shapes, because as before we distinguish between different shapes, and that is only by looking at different compositions. For if the compositions of bodies were equal and similar, their shapes would not differ. His son objected and said: Compositions are homogeneous because two compositions share the most specific attribute of the essence, namely subsisting in two loci, based on his corrupt principle. And if that principle is granted, then there is circularity in this reasoning, because it is possible that compositions are different and share a concomitant accident. What he mentioned as the most specific attribute of composition is only established if compositions are not different, so the premise mentioned in the proof depends on establishing the desired conclusion, which is circularity.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument: al-Jubba'i claims that composition differs with differing shapes. The proof is that we distinguish shapes only by looking at compositions, so if compositions were uniform, shapes would be uniform. His son objects that compositions are homogeneous (mutajānisa) because any two compositions share the attribute of subsisting in two loci, which is the most specific attribute of their essence. He then points out a circularity: the claim that the most specific attribute (subsistence in two loci) is common to all compositions already assumes that compositions are not different, which is what the argument tries to prove. Thus the premise presupposes the conclusion.

Key Terms
تأليف (ta'līf)
شكل (shakl)
متجانسة (mutajānisa)
أخص صفة (akhaṣṣ ṣifa)
مصادرة (muṣādara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Differing shapes directly prove differing compositions regardless of the nature of composition.
Correction: This reasoning assumes that compositions are not essentially homogeneous; the son shows that if compositions share a most specific attribute, the argument is circular.

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Hashim claim that al-Jubba'i's argument is circular?
A) Because al-Jubba'i assumes that compositions are different to prove that shapes are different
B) Because the premise that compositions share a common attribute already presupposes that compositions are not different
C) Because the shapes are not actually different
D) Because the sense of sight cannot perceive composition

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's argument that composition varies with shapes is shown to be circular because the premise claiming a shared attribute of compositions already assumes they are not different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a belief using reasoning that, if honest, assumes the very point you are trying to prove? Where in your understanding of Islamic theology might you be guilty of this?
?
The son's objection exposes circularity in a seemingly plausible argument. Is there an argument you hold about God's attributes or actions that you have never examined for logical consistency? Will you scrutinize it today?
?
If your reasoning about foundational matters is circular, your faith might rest on assumptions rather than evidence. Will you take time to check your core beliefs for logical coherence, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Direct Causation of Composition and Its Indivisibility
Al-Jubba'i claims direct causation; son objects via indivisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām argument that composition can be directly caused by power, and the objection based on composition's indivisibility.

Core Idea
Composition's indivisibility challenges the idea of direct causation by power.

In this short but dense passage, al-Jubba'i presents an argument that composition (like bringing two fingers together) can be directly caused by divine power. His son objects, arguing that composition is indivisible—a single entity subsisting in two loci. This objection raises subtle issues about how we conceive of divine action and the nature of composite entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes a commentary line after 'his saying'.
2
Understand 'composition' (ta'līf)
Recognize that composition here refers to the joining of parts, like bringing two fingers together. Al-Jubba'i uses this as an example of direct causation.
In kalām, 'ta'līf' often means the act of composing or the resulting composition itself.
3
Grasp the son's objection
The son objects that composition is indivisible. If it were divisible, it would entail differentiation (tafrīq). Instead, a single composition can subsist in two loci simultaneously.
This challenges al-Jubba'i's claim because direct causation would seem to require the composition to be a single unified act.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Ask yourself: Why does this matter for divine power? If composition is indivisible, how can God directly cause it without compromising His unity or the nature of composition? This is a key point in the debate about God's attributes and actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سادسها : قال الجبائي : التأليف قد يقع مباشراً ) بالقدرة ( كمن يضم أصبعيه ومنعه قوله : (فلم) لا يجوز إلخ) مذهبه عدم انقسام التأليف لأنه يستلزم انتفاء التفريق ولذا قال بقيام تأليف واحد بمحلين.
The sixth: al-Jubba'i said: Composition may occur directly by power, like one who brings his two fingers together. And he (his son) objected. His saying: '(so why) is it not allowed, etc.' - his doctrine is that composition is indivisible, because it would entail the negation of differentiation, and therefore he said that a single composition subsists in two loci.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This is the sixth argument: al-Jubba'i asserts that composition can be directly caused by power (qudra), giving the example of bringing two fingers together. His son objects, but the text only provides the commentator's note: the son's doctrine holds that composition is indivisible (lā yanqasim), because dividing it would negate differentiation. Therefore, he maintains that a single composition can subsist in two loci. The objection likely targets the idea that directly causing composition would require the composition to be divisible or perceptible in parts.

Key Terms
مباشرة (mubāshara)
قدرة (qudra)
انقسام (inqisām)
تفريق (tafrīq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think composition is a divisible entity that can be caused in parts, like assembling a physical object.
Correction: The son's doctrine holds that composition is indivisible—it cannot be broken down into smaller compositions. A single composition can subsist in multiple loci, meaning it is a unified relational state, not a composite of parts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the son's doctrine regarding composition?
A) Composition is divisible and can be caused by power directly
B) Composition is indivisible and subsists in two loci
C) Composition is not real and exists only in the mind
D) Composition is caused by power indirectly through an intermediary

Key Takeaway
The son's objection establishes that composition is indivisible, which must be accounted for in any theory of divine causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you experience it as a single, unified action or as a collection of disconnected motions? How would viewing your deed as indivisible change the way you approach it?
?
If composition is indivisible, then your bringing together two things (like planning and executing) is not truly separable. In what area of your life have you been treating your actions as divisible, justifying procrastination or half-heartedness? What would it mean for you to commit to a single, indivisible intention from start to finish?

Abu Hashim's Objection on Perceiving Ta'lif and the Response
Abu Hashim objects that if ta'lif is one, seeing it in one page implies seeing it in the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's objection against the perception of a single composition (ta'lif) spanning two pages, and the counter-response that the perceived composition is limited to each page's own jewels.

Core Idea
One composition seen in one page does not force seeing it in another—perception is specific to each page's internal structure.

In kalam discussions on perception, a key question is whether a single entity like composition (ta'lif) can subsist in multiple substrates. Abu Hashim raises a clever objection: if composition is one and subsists in both the upper and lower page of a body, then seeing it in one page should entail seeing it in the other. The response clarifies that the perception is of the composition of each page's own jewels, not a cross-page composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the rhythm of the argument come through.
Notice the compressed style typical of kalam.
2
Identify the objection's core logic
Abu Hashim argues that if ta'lif is one thing subsisting in two pages, then seeing it in the upper page necessitates seeing it in the lower page. Trace the logical steps.
The key is the unity of ta'lif.
3
Examine the response from al-Jubba'i
The response denies that the perceived composition spans both pages. Instead, only the composition within the upper page's own jewels is perceived. Understand why this blocks the objection.
This is a subtle distinction.
4
Consider the further objection about divisibility
A hypothetical questioner suggests that even if composition is one, it could be divisible in perception, so it may be perceived from one side only. Reflect on how this challenges the necessity of seeing both pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج أبو هاشم على سبيل المعارضة بأن لو رؤي التأليف وهو أمر واحد قائم بالصفحتين من الجسم العليا وما تحتها لرؤي الصفحتان معاً وذلك لأن تأليفاً واحداً قام بكل جزأين من الصفحتين، فإذا رؤي قائماً بالصفحة العليا فقد رؤي قائماً بالصفحة التي تحتها ضرورة اتحاده. وإنما يصح هذا الاحتجاج على أبيه لو لم يقل: إن المدرك جواهر الصفحة العليا، وتأليف جواهرها بعضها مع بعض لا تأليف الصفحتين. يعني أنه لا يقول: إن تأليف جواهر الصفحة العليا مع ما تحتها مدرك حتى ينتهض عليه هذه المعارضة، بل يقول: إن المرئي تأليف جواهر الصفحة العليا فيما بينها. على أن لقائل أن يقول: إذا جاز عندك قيام تأليف واحد بجوهرين، فلم لا يجوز انقسامه بحيث يكون مدركاً من أحد الطرفين دون الآخر، فلا يلزم رؤية الصفحتين معا؟
Abu Hashim argued by way of objection that if the composition (ta'lif) were seen, while it is a single thing subsisting in the two pages (al-safhatan) of the body, the upper and what is beneath it, then the two pages would be seen together, because one composition subsists in each of the two parts of the two pages. So if it is seen subsisting in the upper page, it has been seen subsisting in the page beneath it necessarily due to its unity. This objection is valid only against his father if he did not say: 'That which is perceived is the jewels of the upper page, and the composition of its jewels with each other, not the composition of the two pages.' Meaning he does not say that the composition of the jewels of the upper page with what is beneath it is perceived such that this objection would apply; rather he says: 'That which is seen is the composition of the jewels of the upper page among themselves.' Moreover, someone may say: 'Since you allow that one composition subsists in two substances, why is it not permissible that it be divisible so that it is perceived from one side without the other, and thus the seeing of both pages together is not entailed?'
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam argument and counterargument among the Mu'tazila regarding the perception of composition (ta'lif). Abu Hashim objects to the view that composition is one entity subsisting in multiple substrates. He argues that if composition is one and subsists in both the upper and lower pages of a body, then seeing that composition in the upper page would necessarily entail seeing it in the lower page, resulting in both pages being seen together. The response from his father (al-Jubba'i) denies this entailment: the object of perception is not a single composition spanning both pages, but rather the jewels of the upper page and their internal composition among themselves. The father's position is that only the composition of the jewels within each page is perceived, not a composition uniting the two pages. The lesson then adds a further objection from a hypothetical questioner: if one composition can subsist in two substances, why can't it be divisible such that it is perceived from one side only, thus avoiding the entailment of seeing both pages? This objection challenges the necessity of the original argument.

Key Terms
تأليف (ta'lif)
صفحتان (al-safhatan)
مدرك (mudrik)
جوهر (jawhar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is about the physical impossibility of seeing both pages at once.
Correction: The objection is about logical entailment: if composition is one, then seeing it in one page logically forces seeing it in the other, not about physical impossibility.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Abu Hashim's objection?
A) That composition cannot be perceived at all.
B) That if composition is one, seeing it in one page entails seeing it in the other.
C) That composition is divisible.
D) That the jewels of the upper page are not perceived.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's objection fails because the perceived composition is specific to each page's own jewels, not a single composition spanning pages.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume something is one and the same, do you ever consider that your perspective might be limited to only one aspect of it? How does this lesson challenge you to be more precise in your reasoning about unity and perception?
?
Abu Hashim's objection assumed that a single composition must be perceived fully if perceived at all. In your own life, when you think you have understood a person or a situation fully, are you aware that you might only be perceiving one 'page' while assuming you see the whole? How can you guard against this error?
?
The response points out that the composition perceived is only within the upper page's own jewels. Could it be that some of your judgments about others are based on only one part of their 'composition'? What would it take to see them more accurately?

Fifth Argument: Ta'lif Differs with Shapes and Refutation
Al-Jubba'i argues ta'lif differs with shapes; Abu Hashim refutes with petitio principii.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jubba'i's argument that composition (ta'lif) varies with shapes and Abu Hashim's refutation charging it with petitio principii (circular reasoning).

Core Idea
Abu Hashim exposes circular reasoning in his father's argument about composition.

In this passage, we encounter the fifth Mu'tazilite argument on the nature of composition (ta'lif). Al-Jubba'i claims that compositional differences explain shape differences, but his son, Abu Hashim, dismantles the reasoning by pointing out a logical fallacy and a problematic assumption. Let's examine his critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: Al-Jubba'i's claim, then Abu Hashim's objection and the charge of musadara.
2
Identify the logical structure
Distinguish the premise (compositions share an attribute) from the conclusion (compositions are not different). See how the premise depends on the conclusion.
3
Understand the fallacy of musadara
Abu Hashim argues that even if his father's principle is accepted, the reasoning is circular because the premise assumes what it tries to prove.
This is a specific type of petitio principii.
4
Note the grammatical nuance on the preposition
The commentator explains that 'bi' in 'bikhtilaf al-ashkal' indicates accompaniment, not causation. This subtlety affects the argument's force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامسها: قال الجبائي: التأليف مختلف باختلاف الأشكال لما مر من أنا نفرق بين الأشكال المختلفة وما هو إلا بالنظر إلى التأليفات المختلفة، فإنه لو قدر التساوي والتشابه في تأليفات الأجسام لما اختلفت أشكالها. ومنعه ابنه وقال: إن التأليفات متجانسة لأن التأليفين مشتركان في أخص صفة النفس، وهو القيام بمحلين بناء على أصله الفاسد. وإن سلم ذلك الأصل ففيه أي في هذا الاستدلال مصادرة لأنه يجوز أن تكون التأليفات مختلفة ومشتركة في عارض يلزمها، كون ما ذكره من أخص صفات التأليف، إنما يثبت إذا لم تكن التأليفات مختلفة فالمقدمة المذكورة في الدليل متوقفة على ثبوت المطلوب وهو المصادرة. قوله: (باختلاف الأشكال) الباء للملابسة أي حال تلبسها باختلاف الأشكال لا للسببية إذ السببية بالعكس يدل عليه بيان الشارح.
Fifth: Al-Jubba'i said: Composition (ta'lif) differs according to the difference of shapes, because we distinguish between different shapes, and that is only by considering the different compositions. For if equality and similarity in the compositions of bodies were supposed, their shapes would not differ. His son (Abu Hashim) prevented him and said: Compositions are homogeneous because two compositions share the most specific attribute of the soul (al-nafs), which is subsisting in two places [؟] based on his corrupt principle. And if that principle is granted, then there is in this reasoning a petitio principii (musadara), because it is possible that compositions are different and share an accidental attribute that accompanies them. What he mentioned as the most specific attribute of composition is only established if compositions are not different, so the premise mentioned in the proof depends on establishing the desired conclusion, which is petitio principii. His saying: 'according to the difference of shapes' – the preposition 'ba' is for accompaniment, i.e., in the state of their being clothed with difference of shapes, not for causation, since causation is the reverse, as indicated by the explanation of the commentator.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument among the Mu'tazila on the nature of composition (ta'lif). Al-Jubba'i argues that composition varies with the shapes of bodies, because our ability to distinguish different shapes is based on differences in composition; if compositions were the same, shapes would be indistinguishable. His son, Abu Hashim, refutes this by asserting that all compositions are homogeneous, sharing the most specific attribute of the soul (nafs) – subsisting in two loci – based on his father's own (supposedly corrupt) principle. Even if that principle is accepted, the argument commits the fallacy of petitio principii (musadara): the premise that compositions share this attribute depends on the very conclusion that compositions are not different, making the reasoning circular. The passage includes a grammatical gloss on the phrase 'bikhtilaf al-ashkal', clarifying that the preposition 'ba' denotes accompaniment (the composition accompanies the shape difference) rather than causation.

Key Terms
تأليف (ta'lif)
شكل (shakl)
مصادرة (musadara)
أخص صفة النفس (akhass sifat al-nafs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The charge of petitio principii is about a general logical error like assuming the conclusion broadly.
Correction: Abu Hashim's objection is specific: the premise that compositions share the most specific attribute only holds if compositions are not different, which is the very conclusion being argued.

Checkpoint
What fallacy does Abu Hashim charge against his father's argument?
A) Equivocation
B) Petitio principii (circular reasoning)
C) False analogy
D) Argument from ignorance

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's argument that composition varies with shapes is refuted by Abu Hashim as circular reasoning (musadara) because it assumes what it tries to prove.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue for your beliefs, do you ever assume the very thing you are trying to prove? How can you guard against that subtle self-deception?
?
Abu Hashim was willing to grant his father's premise to test its implications. Are you willing to temporarily accept an opponent's view to examine your own position honestly, even if it challenges you?
?
This passage exposes circular reasoning in a theological debate. In your own life, where might you be using circular logic to justify a habit or opinion, and what would it take to break that cycle?

Sixth Argument: Direct Ta'lif by Power and Refutation
Al-Jubba'i claims direct ta'lif; his son refutes via generated causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili disagreement about composition (ta'lif) and the Ash'ari critique of generated causality.

Core Idea
Can power directly produce composition? Mu'tazili and Ash'ari answers differ.

This lesson examines the sixth argument in a series, where al-Jubba'i claims that composition (ta'lif) can be directly produced by power, using the example of bringing fingers together. His son Abu Hashim refutes this by appealing to the Mu'tazili principle of generated effects (mutawallad). The passage also hints at the Ash'ari rejection of that principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim by al-Jubba'i
Al-Jubba'i argues that ta'lif can occur directly by power, like pressing fingers together. Note what 'directly' means in this context.
2
Examine Abu Hashim's refutation
His son argues that composition cannot occur without adjacency (mujawara) that generates it. This is a key Mu'tazili doctrine about generated effects.
3
Identify the underlying principle
The refutation relies on the Mu'tazili agreement that generated effects (mutawallad) are not direct. Understand what 'mutawallad' means in kalam.
4
Contrast with the Ash'ari position
The passage notes that the Ash'ari school rejects this reasoning, holding that power can directly create effects. Reflect on why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سادسها: قال الجبائي: التأليف قد يقع مباشراً بالقدرة كمن يضم أصبعيه. ومنعه ابنه إذ يمتنع وقوع التأليف دون المجاورة المولدة له. وهذا لازم على الجبائي لاتفاق المعتزلة على أن المتولد من السبب لا يكون مباشراً بالقدر الحادثة دون توسط السبب، وإن كان ذلك باطلاً على أصول أصحابنا.
Sixth: Al-Jubba'i said: Composition (ta'lif) may occur directly by power, like one who brings his fingers together. His son prevented him, because the occurrence of composition without the adjacency (mujawara) that generates it is impossible. And this is necessary upon al-Jubba'i due to the agreement of the Mu'tazila that that which is generated (mutawallad) from a cause is not directly [caused] by the originating power without the mediation of the cause, even though that is false according to the principles of our [Ash'ari] school.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument, where al-Jubba'i claims that composition (ta'lif) can be produced directly by power, giving the example of bringing one's fingers together. His son Abu Hashim refutes this by arguing that composition cannot occur without the adjacency (mujawara) that generates it. This refutation is presented as a necessary consequence of al-Jubba'i's own Mu'tazili principles, since the Mu'tazila agree that a generated effect (mutawallad) is not directly caused by the originating power but requires the mediation of a cause. The lesson notes that although this is consistent within Mu'tazili thought, it is false according to the Ash'ari school (the author's own school), which holds that power can directly create effects without necessitating mediation.

Key Terms
تأليف (ta'lif)
مباشر (mubashir)
قدرة (qudra)
مجاورة (mujawara)
متولد (mutawallad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that composition cannot be direct at all.
Correction: The objection is that on Mu'tazili principles, a generated effect (like composition from adjacency) cannot be directly caused by power; it must be mediated. Directness is not impossible per se, but inconsistent with Mu'tazili causality.

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Hashim reject al-Jubba'i's claim that ta'lif can be direct by power?
A) Because composition cannot be caused at all.
B) Because it requires the adjacency that generates it, and generated effects are not direct.
C) Because power cannot produce composition directly.
D) Because the example of fingers is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's claim of direct ta'lif is refuted by his son based on the Mu'tazili doctrine that generated effects require mediation, while Ash'arites hold that power can directly create.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jubba'i's son corrected his own father based on the principles he believed were true. Is there a belief or practice you have inherited that you have never questioned because it comes from someone you respect? Will you examine it today by the Qur'an and Sunnah, even if it means going against a loved one?
?
The Ash'ari position affirms that Allah's power is direct and unmediated. In what area of your life do you rely more on created means than on Allah's direct power? What is one step you can take this week to trust Him more directly in that area?
?
The Mu'tazili commitment to a system of secondary causality led them to reject direct power in this case. Are you holding onto any intellectual system—political, philosophical, or cultural—that limits your understanding of Allah's absolute power? Will you repent from letting any framework override revelation?

The Mu'tazila Argument Against Wet-Dry Adjacency as a Condition for Ta'lif
Most Mu'tazila argue wet-dry adjacency is not a condition for ta'lif; counterexamples given.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that wet-dry adjacency is not a condition for ta'lif, and why they use pearls and rocks as a counterexample.

Core Idea
Wet-dry adjacency is not a condition for composition because it would have to be both initially and continually, but strong composition exists without it.

The seventh argument addresses a key question in kalam physics: is the adjacency of wet and dry substances a necessary condition for the union of atoms (ta'lif)? Most Mu'tazila argue that it is not, presenting a logical reasoning and a striking counterexample from the natural world. Let's examine their case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the claim and reasoning
Find the central claim (that adjacency is not a condition) and the reason given (if condition for initiation, then also for continuance).
The reasoning is based on logical entailment.
3
Examine the counterexample
Observe the counterexample of pearls and solid rocks that have no wetness yet strong composition. Ask yourself how this undermines the conditionality.
This contradicts the entailment, because if adjacency were a condition, it would be needed for continuance, but these objects lack it.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider the overall argument form: it is a reductio ad absurdum against the idea that adjacency is a condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سابعها: ذهب أكثر المعتزلة إلى أن مجاورة الجوهر الرطب والجوهر اليابس وإن ولدت التأليف بينهما كما مر فليست المجاورة المذكورة شرطاً له لأنها لو كانت شرطاً للابتداء أي شرطاً للتأليف في ابتداء حدوثه لكانت شرطاً له في الدوام كأصل المجاورة فإنه شرط للتأليف ابتداء ودواماً. وليس الأمر كذلك كاليواقيت والصخور الصم الصلاب ونحوها فإنها لا رطوبة فيها أصلاً مع قوة التأليف فيما بين جواهرها. وهو أي هذا الاستدلال منقوض بالقدرة فإن تعلقها بالمقدور عندهم شرط لوجوده ابتداء لا دواماً. ومنهم من قال: إنها أي المجاورة بين الرطب واليابس شرط للدوران فإن التأليف الذي يصعب معه الفك والتجزئة لا يتحقق بدون الرطوبة واليبوسة ويتحقق معهما، فهذا التأليف دائر مع المجاورة المذكورة وجوداً وعدماً فهي شرط له. ومع ضعفه أي ضعف الدوران وعدم على أن المدار شرط للدائر فلعل ذلك أي الاختلاف بين المتجاورات في صعوبة التفكيك والتجزئة عائد إلى اختلاف أجناس التأليف كما ذهب إليه الجبائي لا إلى رطوبة بعض الجواهر المتجاورة ويبوسة بعضها.
Seventh: Most of the Mu'tazila held that the adjacency (mujawara) of the wet substance and the dry substance, even if it generates composition (ta'lif) between them as previously mentioned, is not a condition for it. Because if it were a condition for initiation (al-ibtida'), i.e., a condition for the composition at the beginning of its occurrence, it would be a condition for it in continuance (al-dawam) like the original adjacency, for it is a condition for composition initially and continually. But the matter is not so, like pearls and solid rocks and the like, for they have no wetness at all while the composition among their jewels is strong. And this argument is refuted by power (al-qudra), for its attachment to the capable object according to them is a condition for its existence initially but not continually. And some of them said: The adjacency between the wet and dry is a condition by correlation (al-dawaran), for the composition that is difficult to separate and disintegrate is not realized without wetness and dryness and is realized with them. Thus this composition rotates with the mentioned adjacency in existence and non-existence, so it is a condition for it. And despite its weakness, i.e., the weakness of the correlation and the fact that the rotating thing is a condition for the correlate, perhaps the difference among adjacent things in difficulty of separation and disintegration is due to the difference in genera of composition, as al-Jubba'i held, not to the wetness of some adjacent jewels and the dryness of others.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument regarding whether the adjacency of wet and dry substances is a condition for composition (ta'lif). Most Mu'tazila argue that although such adjacency generates composition, it is not a condition for it. Their reasoning: if it were a condition for the initiation of composition, it would also be a condition for its continuance, just as the original adjacency (the mere contact) is a condition for both initiation and continuance. But this is not the case, as seen in pearls and solid rocks, which have no wetness yet possess strong composition. The lesson then notes a refutation: the same logic would imply that power (qudra) must be a condition for both initiation and continuance of the act, but Mu'tazila themselves hold power is a condition only for initiation. A second view is presented: some hold that wet-dry adjacency is a condition by correlation (dawaran), meaning strong composition (difficult to separate) always accompanies wetness and dryness, so it is a condition. However, the passage notes the weakness of this correlation argument, and suggests that differences in difficulty of separation may be due to the genus of composition itself, as al-Jubba'i argued, rather than to wetness and dryness.

Key Terms
مجاورة
تأليف
شرط
ابتداء
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Mu'tazila deny that wet-dry adjacency generates composition at all.
Correction: The passage clearly states that adjacency 'generates composition' (تولد التأليف); they only deny it is a necessary condition (شرط).

Checkpoint
What counterexample do most Mu'tazila use to argue that wet-dry adjacency is not a condition for composition?
A) The case of fire burning
B) Pearls and solid rocks which have no wetness yet have strong composition
C) The movement of a hand
D) The generation of heat from the sun

Key Takeaway
Most Mu'tazila hold that wet-dry adjacency is not a necessary condition for ta'lif, as shown by examples like pearls and rocks that have strong composition without wetness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat any religious practice or experience as a necessary condition for your spiritual life, even though Allah may be working through other means? Examine if you have 'pearls'—moments of strong faith without that condition. Will you let go of assumptions that limit how you expect Allah to work?
?
The Mu'tazila used empirical observation (pearls and rocks) to challenge a theoretical condition. When did you last allow reality to challenge a belief you held about your deen? Are you willing to test your assumptions against concrete examples from life, even if it unsettles you?
?
Strong composition exists without wetness in the atoms of rocks. What does this tell you about the power and wisdom of the Creator? Can you think of an area in your life where you insist on a 'wetness' (a certain feeling, circumstance) for your faith to be strong, but Allah has given you strength anyway? How can you thank Him for that and grow in trust?

Refutation and Alternative Views on the Conditionality of Ta'lif
Refutation of main argument and presentation of alternative correlation view with rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the Mu'tazila argument using qudra, the alternative view that adjacency is a condition by correlation, and its rebuttal.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own principle about qudra undermines their argument; correlation attempts to re-establish conditionality but is found weak.

Having argued that wet-dry adjacency is not a condition for ta'lif, the Mu'tazila's own reasoning is criticized using their own doctrine of qudra. Then an alternative view emerges: some propose that adjacency is a condition by correlation (dawaran). However, this view is also challenged. This lesson traces these subsequent moves.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation using qudra
Read the first part of the passage carefully. The author points out that the Mu'tazila's argument is refuted by their own view of qudra (power).
The refutation is a tuqid (counter-argument) showing inconsistency.
2
Understand the alternative correlation argument
Then some Mu'tazila propose a different way: adjacency is a condition by correlation (dawaran). Grasp how this argument works by existence and non-existence.
Correlation (dawaran) is a method to infer conditionality.
3
Examine the rebuttal of the correlation
The author notes the weakness of the correlation argument and suggests that differences in difficulty of separation are due to the genus of composition (al-Jubba'i's view), not wetness.
This rebuttal implies correlation does not prove causation.
4
Connect to al-Jubba'i's position
Consider how al-Jubba'i's explanation (differences in ta'lif genera) offers an alternative to wet-dry dependency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
