Defining motion as a first perfection in a specific aspect
Al-Jurjani defines motion and distinguishes it from absolute first perfections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion is classified as a first perfection only in a specific aspect, distinguishing it from other perfections like forms.

Core Idea
Motion is a first perfection only relative to a second perfection, not absolutely.

Al-Jurjani begins by situating motion within the broader scheme of perfections. He distinguishes between first perfections that are absolute (like forms) and those that are relative (like motion). This passage clarifies that motion's firstness is conditional upon its leading to another perfection, which is why we need the restriction of the aspect (haythiyya) in the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two restrictions
The passage uses two restrictions: 'by the restriction of firstness' and 'by the restriction of the aspect'. Notice what each is intended to exclude.
2
Identify the absolute first perfections
The specific forms (suwar naw'iyya) and corporeal forms (suwar jismiyya) are first perfections absolutely. Understand why they are not restricted by aspect.
3
Understand motion's unique status
Motion is a first perfection only in a specific aspect because it entails a subsequent perfection. Compare this to the absolute first perfections.
Motion is actually a second perfection in relation to forms, but first in relation to its goal.
4
Reflect on the phrase 'not absolutely'
The phrase 'not absolutely' at the end is crucial. It emphasizes that motion's firstness is conditional, unlike forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الأول : رأي الحكماء في الجسم
من الكمال، وفيما يتأدى إليه ذلك الكمال وبقيد الأولية تخرج الكمالات الثانية، وبقيد الحيثية المتعلقة بالأولى تخرج الكمالات الأولى على الإطلاق، أعني الصور النوعية لأنواع الأجسام والصور الجسمية للجسم المطلق، فإنها كمالات أولى لما بالقوة لكن لا من هذه الحيثية بل مطلقاً لأنَّ تحصل هذه الأنواع، والجسم المطلق في أنفسها إنما هو بهذه الصور وما عداها من أحوالها تابعة لها بخلاف الحركة، فإنها كمال أول هذه الحيثية فقط، وذلك لأنَّ الحركة في الحقيقة من الكمالات الثانية بالقياس إلى الصور النوعية والجسمية وإنما اتصف بالأولية لاستلزامها ترتب كمال آخر عليها، بحيث يجب كونه بالقوة معها فهي أول بالقياس إلى ذلك الكمال وكونه بالقوة معها لا مطلقاً ( وكونه) أي كون المتحرك (بالقوة ) إنما هو (باعتبار عارض للمتحرك ) وذلك العارض هو الكمال الثاني المقصود حصوله بالحركة ونفس الحركة أيضاً، فإنَّ المتحرك موصوف بالقوة باعتبار هذين العارضين لا باعتبار ذاته بالفعل في صورته الجسمية والنوعية فلا يصح أن يقال : لما بالقوة، ويراد أنَّ محل الحركة بالقوة في ذاته لأنه إذا كان بالقوة في ذاته لم يتصور اتصافه بالحركة، فقوله : لما بالقوة معناه لما هو بالقوة في شيء من عوارضه لا في ذاته ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يرد به هذا المعنى ( فهو ) أي المتحرك ) كمال ) أي بحسب ذاته وصورته (أيضاً) كما لا بحسب حركته، والمقصود أنه إن لم يرد به كونه بالقوة في عارضه بل أريد كونه بالقوة في ذاته لم يصح لأنه ليس بالقوة في ذاته، بل بالفعل ) فلذلك ) أي فلأنَّ كونه بالقوة، إنما هو باعتبار عارض ( اعتبرنا الحيثية ) إذ لو أريد كونه بالقوة في ذاته كما يتبادر من العبارة، لم يكن لاعتبار الحيثية معنى وحاصل ما ذكره أن قيد الحيثية يفيد أنَّ القوة بحسب العارض دون الذات، وذلك لا ينافي كونه احترازاً عما ذكرناه .
قوله : ( في ذلك الكمال إلخ) أجرى بالقوة على إطلاقه كما هو المتبادر، فيخرج الكون فإنَّ الصورة الأولى كمال أول لا بالقوة في الكمال الثاني وهو الصورة الثانية، لكن ليست كمالاً فيما هو بالقوة في تلك الصورة، وبهذا ظهر أن تخصيص القوة بما يتأدى إليه كما في شرح التجريد كونه تخصيصاً من غير مخصص محل لمنع التعريف .
قوله : (تخرج) الكمالات (الثانية أي من حيث أنها كمالات ثانية .
قوله : (إنما هو بهذه الصور هذه كمالات ذاتية تم بها ذوات الجسم والأنواع بعد كونها ناقصة بدون اعتبار تلك الصور وليست الهيولى كمالاً لنفسها وهو ظاهر ولا لذوات الجسم والأنواع، إذ لا يتصور وجود شيء منها بدون الهيولى إلا باعتبار نفسها ولا باعتبار جزئها، فتدبر فإنه مما زل فيه الأقدام .
The Fourth Observatory - First Intention: The Opinion of the Philosophers Regarding the Body. From perfection, and in what that perfection leads to; and by the restriction of firstness, the second perfections are excluded; and by the restriction of the aspect (al-haythiyya) related to the first, the first perfections absolutely are excluded, namely the specific forms (al-suwar al-naw'iyya) of the types of bodies and the corporeal forms (al-suwar al-jismiyya) of the absolute body. For they are first perfections for that which is in potentiality, but not from this aspect; rather absolutely, because the attainment of these types and the absolute body in themselves is only through these forms; and other than them of their states are subordinate to them. Unlike motion, for it is a first perfection only from this aspect. And that is because motion in reality is among the second perfections in relation to the specific and corporeal forms; but it is characterized by firstness because it entails the arrangement of another perfection upon it, such that it must be in potentiality with it. So it is first in relation to that perfection and its being in potentiality with it, not absolutely. (And his being: i.e., the being of the moving thing (in potentiality) is only (by consideration of an accident of the moving thing). That accident is the second perfection intended to be obtained by the motion, and also the motion itself. For the moving thing is described as in potentiality with respect to these two accidents, not with respect to its essence being actual in its corporeal and specific form. So it is not correct to say 'that which is in potentiality' intending that the locus of motion is in potentiality in its essence, because if it were in potentiality in its essence, its being described by motion would not be conceivable. So his statement: 'that which is in potentiality' means that which is in potentiality in some of its accidents, not in its essence. (Otherwise) i.e., if this meaning is not intended, (then it is) i.e., the moving thing (is a perfection) i.e., according to its essence and its form (also) just as [it is] not according to its motion. The intended meaning is that if it is not taken as being in potentiality in its accident, but rather as being in potentiality in its essence, it is not correct because it is not in potentiality in its essence, but rather actual. (Therefore) i.e., because its being in potentiality is only by consideration of an accident (we considered the aspect). For if its being in potentiality in its essence were intended, as is the immediate understanding of the expression, then considering the aspect would have no meaning. The upshot of what he mentioned is that the restriction of the aspect indicates that potentiality is with respect to the accident, not the essence; and that does not contradict its being a precaution from what we mentioned. His statement: (regarding that perfection, etc.) He applied 'in potentiality' in its absolute sense, as is the immediate understanding; so it excludes generation (al-kawn), for the first form is a first perfection not in potentiality in the second perfection, which is the second form; but it is not a perfection in that which is in potentiality in that form. By this it becomes clear that specifying potentiality to that which is attained by it, as in the commentary on al-Tajrid, is a specification without a specifier, a place for preventing the definition. His statement: (It excludes) the perfections (the second), i.e., from the perspective that they are second perfections. His statement: (It is only by these forms) These are essential perfections by which the essences of the body and the types are completed after they were deficient without consideration of those forms. And the hyle (hayula) is not a perfection for itself, which is clear, nor for the essences of the body and types, because the existence of something without hyle is not conceivable except by considering itself and not considering its part. So ponder, for it is one of those things in which feet have slipped.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines motion as a first perfection (al-kamal al-awwal) and clarifies the crucial condition of the aspect (al-haythiyya) regarding potentiality. The commentator first sets up the definition of motion within the broader classification of perfections: first perfections are those that make a potential being actual, while second perfections depend on a prior perfection. He explains that motion is a first perfection only in a specific aspect, not absolutely, because its being in potentiality is relative to a second perfection (the attainment of a state). The commentator then emphasizes that the moving thing is in potentiality only by virtue of an accident (such as the second perfection), not in its essence. This leads to the necessity of including the restriction of the aspect (al-haythiyya) in the definition to avoid misunderstanding. The subsequent commentaries reinforce this by explaining that if potentiality were taken in an absolute sense, the definition would fail. The commentator also addresses a potential objection from the commentary on al-Tajrid regarding the specification of potentiality.

The necessity of aspectual potentiality in motion
Al-Jurjani explains the condition of aspectual potentiality and criticizes an alternative definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the moving thing's potentiality must be in an accident, not its essence, and why the aspect (haythiyya) is necessary in the definition.

Core Idea
Potentiality in motion is in the accident, not the essence; otherwise motion would be impossible.

Having established that motion is a first perfection only in a specific aspect, al-Jurjani now explains why that aspect is necessary. He argues that if potentiality were taken as referring to the essence, the moving thing would be in potentiality in its very being, which would preclude motion. Therefore, potentiality must be understood as pertaining to an accident—the second perfection. This leads to the inclusion of the aspect (haythiyya) in the definition to avoid misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two accidents mentioned
The passage states that the moving thing is in potentiality by consideration of an accident, which is the second perfection and also the motion itself.
2
Understand the consequence of essential potentiality
If the moving thing were in potentiality in its essence, it would be pure potentiality and could not be described as having motion.
This is a key logical point.
3
Grasp the role of the aspect
The aspect (haythiyya) is needed to indicate that the potentiality is in an accident, not the essence. Without it, the definition would be misunderstood.
4
Consider the critique of al-Tajrid
Al-Jurjani points out that specifying potentiality to what is attained by it (as in the commentary on al-Tajrid) is arbitrary and not justified.
This reinforces the need for the aspect.

من الكمال، وفيما يتأدى إليه ذلك الكمال وبقيد الأولية تخرج الكمالات الثانية، وبقيد الحيثية المتعلقة بالأولى تخرج الكمالات الأولى على الإطلاق، أعني الصور النوعية لأنواع الأجسام والصور الجسمية للجسم المطلق، فإنها كمالات أولى لما بالقوة لكن لا من هذه الحيثية بل مطلقاً لأنَّ تحصل هذه الأنواع، والجسم المطلق في أنفسها إنما هو بهذه الصور وما عداها من أحوالها تابعة لها بخلاف الحركة، فإنها كمال أول هذه الحيثية فقط، وذلك لأنَّ الحركة في الحقيقة من الكمالات الثانية بالقياس إلى الصور النوعية والجسمية وإنما اتصف بالأولية لاستلزامها ترتب كمال آخر عليها، بحيث يجب كونه بالقوة معها فهي أول بالقياس إلى ذلك الكمال وكونه بالقوة معها لا مطلقاً ( وكونه) أي كون المتحرك (بالقوة ) إنما هو (باعتبار عارض للمتحرك ) وذلك العارض هو الكمال الثاني المقصود حصوله بالحركة ونفس الحركة أيضاً، فإنَّ المتحرك موصوف بالقوة باعتبار هذين العارضين لا باعتبار ذاته بالفعل في صورته الجسمية والنوعية فلا يصح أن يقال : لما بالقوة، ويراد أنَّ محل الحركة بالقوة في ذاته لأنه إذا كان بالقوة في ذاته لم يتصور اتصافه بالحركة، فقوله : لما بالقوة معناه لما هو بالقوة في شيء من عوارضه لا في ذاته ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يرد به هذا المعنى ( فهو ) أي المتحرك ) كمال ) أي بحسب ذاته وصورته (أيضاً) كما لا بحسب حركته، والمقصود أنه إن لم يرد به كونه بالقوة في عارضه بل أريد كونه بالقوة في ذاته لم يصح لأنه ليس بالقوة في ذاته، بل بالفعل ) فلذلك ) أي فلأنَّ كونه بالقوة، إنما هو باعتبار عارض ( اعتبرنا الحيثية ) إذ لو أريد كونه بالقوة في ذاته كما يتبادر من العبارة، لم يكن لاعتبار الحيثية معنى وحاصل ما ذكره أن قيد الحيثية يفيد أنَّ القوة بحسب العارض دون الذات، وذلك لا ينافي كونه احترازاً عما ذكرناه .
قوله : ( في ذلك الكمال إلخ) أجرى بالقوة على إطلاقه كما هو المتبادر، فيخرج الكون فإنَّ الصورة الأولى كمال أول لا بالقوة في الكمال الثاني وهو الصورة الثانية، لكن ليست كمالاً فيما هو بالقوة في تلك الصورة، وبهذا ظهر أن تخصيص القوة بما يتأدى إليه كما في شرح التجريد كونه تخصيصاً من غير مخصص محل لمنع التعريف .

The Fourth Observatory - First Intention: The Opinion of the Philosophers Regarding the Body. From perfection, and in what that perfection leads to; and by the restriction of firstness, the second perfections are excluded; and by the restriction of the aspect (al-haythiyya) related to the first, the first perfections absolutely are excluded, namely the specific forms (al-suwar al-naw'iyya) of the types of bodies and the corporeal forms (al-suwar al-jismiyya) of the absolute body. For they are first perfections for that which is in potentiality, but not from this aspect; rather absolutely, because the attainment of these types and the absolute body in themselves is only through these forms; and other than them of their states are subordinate to them. Unlike motion, for it is a first perfection only from this aspect. And that is because motion in reality is among the second perfections in relation to the specific and corporeal forms; but it is characterized by firstness because it entails the arrangement of another perfection upon it, such that it must be in potentiality with it. So it is first in relation to that perfection and its being in potentiality with it, not absolutely. (And his being: i.e., the being of the moving thing (in potentiality) is only (by consideration of an accident of the moving thing). That accident is the second perfection intended to be obtained by the motion, and also the motion itself. For the moving thing is described as in potentiality with respect to these two accidents, not with respect to its essence being actual in its corporeal and specific form. So it is not correct to say 'that which is in potentiality' intending that the locus of motion is in potentiality in its essence, because if it were in potentiality in its essence, its being described by motion would not be conceivable. So his statement: 'that which is in potentiality' means that which is in potentiality in some of its accidents, not in its essence. (Otherwise) i.e., if this meaning is not intended, (then it is) i.e., the moving thing (is a perfection) i.e., according to its essence and its form (also) just as [it is] not according to its motion. The intended meaning is that if it is not taken as being in potentiality in its accident, but rather as being in potentiality in its essence, it is not correct because it is not in potentiality in its essence, but rather actual. (Therefore) i.e., because its being in potentiality is only by consideration of an accident (we considered the aspect). For if its being in potentiality in its essence were intended, as is the immediate understanding of the expression, then considering the aspect would have no meaning. The upshot of what he mentioned is that the restriction of the aspect indicates that potentiality is with respect to the accident, not the essence; and that does not contradict its being a precaution from what we mentioned. His statement: (regarding that perfection, etc.) He applied 'in potentiality' in its absolute sense, as is the immediate understanding; so it excludes generation (al-kawn), for the first form is a first perfection not in potentiality in the second perfection, which is the second form; but it is not a perfection in that which is in potentiality in that form. By this it becomes clear that specifying potentiality to that which is attained by it, as in the commentary on al-Tajrid, is a specification without a specifier, a place for preventing the definition. His statement: (It excludes) the perfections (the second), i.e., from the perspective that they are second perfections. His statement: (It is only by these forms) These are essential perfections by which the essences of the body and the types are completed after they were deficient without consideration of those forms. And the hyle (hayula) is not a perfection for itself, which is clear, nor for the essences of the body and types, because the existence of something without hyle is not conceivable except by considering itself and not considering its part. So ponder, for it is one of those things in which feet have slipped.

Applying the Definition of First Perfection to Circular Motion
Al-Jurjani discusses applying the definition of first perfection to eternal circular motion and resolves the objection by considering a hypothetical terminus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of motion as a first perfection applies to eternal circular motion through a hypothetical terminus.

Core Idea
Circular motion's lack of an actual end does not prevent it from being a first perfection.

The definition of motion as a first perfection seems straightforward for motions with a clear beginning and end. But what about circular motion, which has no terminus? Al-Jurjani addresses this objection and shows how a hypothetical point can serve as a relative terminus, preserving the definition's validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the passage where the author states the problem: circular motion has no real terminus, so how can it have a first perfection?
The objection is that circular motion has no actual end, so the notions of first and second perfection do not apply.
2
Understand the solution
Notice that the solution employs a hypothetical point—imagine a position on the circle, and consider the motion before it as first perfection relative to that point.
This relative terminus is purely conceptual, not real.
3
Compare to linear motion
The commentator likens the hypothetical point to an assumed boundary in straight motion; both serve as relative termini.
Just as a boundary in linear motion can be assumed, so can a position in circular motion.
4
Address the counter-objection
The critic says the definition implies an actual terminus, not an imagined one. The response clarifies that 'firstness' is relative: if the terminus is assumed, the firstness is similarly assumed.
Thus, there is no flaw in the definition even for circular motion.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The definition is not flawed because the priority can be based on assumption, not on real existence of a terminus.
This shows the flexibility of the definition across all types of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف : ( وفي انطباق هذا الحد على الحركة المستديرة ) الأزلية الأبدية على
زعمهم (نظر إذ لا منتهى لها إلا بالوهم فليس هناك كمالان أول) هو الحركة، ( وثان ) هو الوصول إلى المنتهى، نعم إذا اعتبر وضع من الأوضاع، واعتبر ما قبله دون ما بعده كانت الحركة السابقة كمالاً أول بالقياس إلى ذلك الوضع، إلا أنَّ هذا منتهى بحسب الوهم دون الواقع فيكون بمنزلة ما إذ اعتبر حد من الحدود الواقعة في مسافة الحركة ويجعل ذلك منتهى للحركة السابقة عليه ولا شبهة في أن المتبادر من التعريف أن تكون الحركة كمالاً أول بحسب نفس الأمر، لا بمجرد التوهم فقط،
قوله : ( نعم إذا اعتبر إلخ) الوضع المفروض في الحركة المستديرة كالحد المفروض في الحركة الآنية، فكما أنَّ الحد المفروض لا يصير منتهى الحركة الآنية المتصلة ما لم يفرض أن لا تكون الحركة فيما بعده كذلك الوضع فلذا قال : واعتبر ما قبله دون ما بعده أي اعتبروا الحركة التي قبله .
قوله : ( ولا شبهة في أنَّ المتبادر إلخ) فيه أنَّ قيد الأولوية مشعر بأن أولوية ما بعد للقوة فإنَّ كان بحسب الاعتبار كانت الأولوية بحسب الاعتبار فلا غبار على التعريف .
The author said: (And regarding the compatibility of this definition with circular motion), eternal and everlasting, upon their claim (consideration: for it has no terminus except by imagination, so there are not two perfections: first) which is motion, (and second) which is reaching the terminus. Yes, if a position among positions is considered, and what is before it is considered without what is after it, then the previous motion becomes a first perfection in relation to that position, except that this terminus is according to imagination, not in reality. So it is like when a boundary among the boundaries occurring in the distance of motion is considered, and that is made a terminus for the previous motion. And there is no doubt that the immediate understanding of the definition is that motion is a first perfection according to the actual state of affairs, not merely by imagination only. His statement: (Yes, if considered, etc.) The assumed position in circular motion is like the assumed boundary in continuous motion. For just as the assumed boundary does not become a terminus of the continuous motion unless it is assumed that there is no motion after it, likewise the position. Therefore he said: 'and consider what is before it without what is after it', i.e., consider the motion before it. His statement: (And there is no doubt that the immediate understanding, etc.) In it is that the restriction of firstness indicates that the priority of what is after is [in relation to] potentiality. So if it is according to assumption, then the priority is according to assumption, so there is no flaw in the definition.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that the definition of motion as a first perfection does not apply to eternal circular motion (like the celestial spheres) because such motion has no beginning or end, so there is no second perfection (terminus) to which it leads, and thus no first perfection. The commentator provides the solution: although there is no actual terminus, we can imagine a specific point in the circle and consider the motion before that point as a first perfection relative to that point. This is a permissible assumption. The critic further objects that the definition seems to require an actual terminus, not just an imagined one. The commentator responds that the restriction of 'firstness' in the definition is relative: if the second perfection is assumed, then the firstness is also by assumption, so there is no problem. The definition is not flawed by this.

Objection and Response on the Definition of Motion
Al-Jurjani addresses the claim that the definition of motion is more obscure than motion itself and refutes it by distinguishing between types of conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defend a technical definition against the charge of being more obscure than the thing defined, by distinguishing between different levels of conception.

Core Idea
A definition can be more obscure in one sense yet perfectly valid in another.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary addresses a common objection in logic and kalam: that a definition using technical terms is less clear than the thing it defines. The objection arises from confusing everyday awareness with precise essential definition. Al-Jurjani clarifies the distinction and defends the definition of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector claims that since everyone knows motion, a definition using obscure terms is problematic. Identify the reasoning.
2
Understand the response
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between a vague conception and an essential definition. The definition aims for the latter.
3
Examine the critique
A later commentator argued the response insufficient. Note the counterpoint.
4
Reflect on the nuance
Al-Jurjani adds that something can be conceived in multiple ways, some more manifest, some more obscure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( بأن ما أورده إلخ) أي التفرقة التي أوردها تدل على تصورها بالوجه الذي يتوقف تلك التفرقة والتصديق يوردها للأجسام، ولا يدل على تصور حقيقتها والأمور المذكورة في التعريف يتصور حقيقتها وتصوره بالحقيقة ليس أجلى منها حتى يكون تعريف الشيء بما هو أخفى منها وجهان وجه أجلى لا يمكن تعريفه بالأمور المذكورة وحقيقة هي أخفى من الأمور يمكن تعريفها، وبما حررنا ظهر أن ما في الشرح الجديد للتجريد من أن هذا الجواب لا يشفى العليل إذ لا يدفع المحذور الذي هو التعريف بالأخفى، ويمكن أن يقال : قد يتصور شيء بوجوه بعضها أخفى وقد يورد فيما يحصل صوره بالوجه الخفي أمور هي أخفى من المعرف تلك
قوله : ( وقد أجيب عنه بأن ما أورده إلخ) قيل : هذا الجواب لا يشفي العليل إذ لا يدفع المحذور الذي هو التعريف بالأخفى، ثم قيل : ويمكن أن يقال : قد يتصور شيء بوجوه بعضها أجلى وبعضها أخفى، وقد يورد فيما يحصل به تصوره بالوجه الخفي أمور هي أخفى من المعرف بالوجه الجلي، لكن أجلى من المعرف بهذا الوجه الخفي وأنت خبير بأن حاصل جواب الشارح منع كون تصور كنه الحركة أسهل مما ذكر في التعريف وبيان أن ما ذكر في بيانه من أن كل عاقل إلخ، لا يفيد تلك الأسهلية، بل إنما يفيد تصورها بالوجه فقول المعترض لا يدفع المحذور الذي هو التعريف بالأخفى في حيز المنع ، وأما الجواب الذي ذكره نفسه، فلا يخفى ما فيه من التعسف .
His statement: (By what he mentioned, etc.) i.e., the distinction he mentioned indicates a conception of it in a certain way that depends on that distinction and affirmation of its occurrence in bodies, but does not indicate a conception of its essence. And the matters mentioned in the definition give a conception of its essence, and the conception of the essence is not more evident than them such that defining something by what is more obscure than it [would be problematic]. There are two aspects: one is more evident, but it is not possible to define it by the matters mentioned; and the essence is more obscure than the matters that can define it. By what we have set forth, it becomes clear that what is in the New Commentary on al-Tajrid, that this answer does not heal the sickness, because it does not remove the problem of defining by the more obscure, is [itself problematic]. And it can be said: a thing can be conceived in various ways, some more obscure, and it is possible to bring matters that are more obscure than the thing being defined for the purpose of obtaining a conception of it by the obscure way. His statement: (And it was answered about him by what he mentioned, etc.) It was said: This answer does not heal the sickness, because it does not remove the problem of defining by the more obscure. Then it was said: It can be said: a thing can be conceived in various ways, some more manifest and some more obscure, and it is possible to bring matters that are more obscure than the thing defined by the manifest way, but more manifest than the thing defined by this obscure way. And you are aware that the gist of the commentator's answer is to deny that the conception of the essence of motion is easier than what is mentioned in the definition, and to clarify that what is mentioned in his explanation, that every rational person, etc., does not provide that easiness; rather it only provides a conception of it in a [certain] way. So the objector's claim that it does not remove the problem of defining by the more obscure is subject to denial. And as for the answer that he himself mentioned, there is no denying the awkwardness in it.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection from al-Mulakhkhas that the definition of motion is more obscure than motion itself, since every rational person knows the difference between motion and rest, but the definition uses complex terms like first perfection, potentiality, etc. The commentator responds by distinguishing between two levels of conception: a vague awareness and a precise essential definition. The objection only proves the former, not the latter. The definition aims to give an essential definition, which is necessarily more technical. The commentator also engages with a critique from the New Commentary on al-Tajrid that the response is insufficient, and offers a further nuance: a thing can be conceived in different ways, and what is more obscure in one way may be more manifest in another. The final note acknowledges some awkwardness in the response but defends the definition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A definition must always be simpler or more clear than the thing defined.
Correction: Essential definitions often require technical terms that are less familiar but still necessary to capture the essence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, what does the objector's argument actually demonstrate?
A) That the definition is defective.
B) That people have a vague conception of motion.
C) That motion cannot be defined.
D) That essential definitions are impossible.

Key Takeaway
A definition may be more obscure than its subject in everyday terms yet still be the only way to capture its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objector assumes that because everyone knows motion, a technical definition is useless. In your own journey, have you ever dismissed a deep study of aqeedah thinking you already know enough from common sense? What would it take for you to commit to seeking the essence—haqīqah—of your faith?
?
The commentator admits awkwardness in the response but still defends it. Are you willing to accept that some truths are not perfectly smooth or easy to articulate? How does this patience apply to your learning, especially in areas where you find yourself frustrated by difficulty?

Circularity in Defining Motion as Gradual Change
Presents the definition of motion and the circularity argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the classical definition of motion as gradual change leads to circularity.

Core Idea
Defining motion by gradualness and time is circular.

The philosophers defined motion as an exit from potentiality to actuality gradually. But 'gradualness' depends on 'instant', which depends on 'time', and 'time' is defined as the measure of motion. This creates a circular definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the definition and the circularity argument.
Pay attention to the sequence of concepts.
2
Identify the components
Note the key terms: motion, gradualness, instant, time. How do they depend on each other?
3
Trace the circularity
Follow the reasoning: gradualness requires instant, instant requires time, and time is defined by motion.
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why is this a problem? A definition should not rely on the concept it defines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الأول : رأي الحكماء في الجسم

المعلم الأول، وأتباعه في تحديد الحركة ( قريب مما قاله قدماؤهم ) من ( أنها خروج من القوة إلى الفعل بالتدريج ) فإنهم قالوا : الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل إما أن يكون دفعة أو لا دفعة والثاني : هو المسمى بالحركة فحقيقة الحركة هو الحدوث أو الحصول أو الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل إما يسيراً يسيراً أو لا دفعة أو بالتدريج وكل واحدة من هذه العبارات صالحة لإفادة تصور الحركة ( لكن ) متأخروهم (عدلوا عن ذلك لأنَّ التدريج هو وقوع الشيء في زمان بعد زمان ) بل نقول : هو وقوع الشيء في آن بعد أن فيتوقف تصور التدريج على تصور الآن المتوقف تصوره على تصور الزمان لأنه طرفه، وكذا معنى يسيراً يسيراً هو معنى التدريج وتصور اللادفعة موقوف على وهي عبارة عن الحصول في الآن فإن الأمور الواقعة في تعريف الحركة ينتهي تحليلها إلى تصور الزمان الذي هو مقدار الحركة، فالتعريف دوري وإلى هذا أشار بقوله : ( فيقع في تعريفه ) أي تعريف التدريج ( الزمان وهو يعرف بأنه مقدار الحركة فيلزم الدور) قال الإمام الرازي : أجاب بعض الفضلاء عن ذلك بأن تصور الدفعة بالوجه الجلي لكنها أجلى من المعرف بهذا الوجه أخفي انتهى ناشئ من قلة التدبر، لما عرفت أنَّ حاصل الجواب المذكور ما ذكره بقوله : ويمكن أن يقال إلخ أفراد والحال قبول الثاني ليس بشيء. قوله : ( لأن وقوعه إلخ) ليس المراد ما هو الظاهر المتبادر من العبارة إذ يقع الشيء مرتين مع أنه ليس بتدريج، بل المراد أن لا شيء المتصل في تفسير المطلق. قوله : ( على تصور (الزمان) فإذا فرضه العقل منقسماً بعد الجزأين المتحدين مع الكل في الحقيقة، والاسم متقدماً على الآخر بحسب الزمان وذلك الشيء واقع بالتدريج، أي لا يمكن وجوده بحيث تكون أجزاؤه المفروضة مجتمعة، وهذا في الحركة بمعنى القطع وما في الحركة بمعنى التوسط فوقوعه بالتدريج وهو وقوعه في آن بالنسبة إلى حدي المسافة بعد وقوعه في آن آخر، فتدريجه باعتبار النسب العارضة لها، أي لا يمكن حصوله في حدود المسافة وأما باعتبار ذاته فدفعي وبما حررنا من معنى التدريج اندفع الشبهة التي أوردها الإمام في المباحث المشرقية، حيث قال لي في التدريج شك لأن التدريج لا يكون بدون تغير، والتغير إنما يكون لحدوث شيء أو زوال شيء لشيء حادث آني، وأن يحصل ابتداء وجوده إن لم يحصل بتمامه فهو حاصل دفعة لأن ابتداء الحوادث آني وإن لم يحصل بتمامه فذلك الذي بقي غير الذي حصل لامتناع أن يكون الشيء الواحد موجوداً ومعدوماً دفعه، فليس هناك شيء واحد له حصول على التدريج، بل هناك أمور متتالية فالحاصل أن الشيء الواحدي الذات يمتنع أن يكون له حصول إلا دفعة، نعم الشيء الذي له أجزاء كثيرة أمكن أن يقال : إِنَّ حصوله على التدريج على معنى كل

قوله : ( فيلزم الدور) قد يقال : التدريج الواقع في تعريف الزمان هو التدريج اللغوي المفسر بالزمان اللغوي الأعم مما عرفه أرسطو، فلا محذور وأنت خبير بأنه قريب مما ذكره الإمام .
The Fourth Observatory – First Objective: The opinion of the philosophers concerning the body.

The First Teacher and his followers in defining motion (close to what their predecessors said) from (that it is an exit from potentiality to actuality gradually). For they said: The exit from potentiality to actuality is either instantaneous or not instantaneous, and the second is called motion. So the reality of motion is occurrence or obtaining or exit from potentiality to actuality either little by little or not instantaneously or gradually. And each of these expressions is suitable for giving the concept of motion. (But) their later followers (changed from that because gradualness is the occurrence of a thing in time after time). Rather we say: It is the occurrence of a thing in an instant after an instant. Thus the conception of gradualness depends on the conception of the instant, whose conception depends on the conception of time because it is its extremity. Likewise the meaning of 'little by little' is the meaning of gradualness. And the conception of 'not instantaneous' depends on it, and it is an expression for obtaining in the instant. For the matters occurring in the definition of motion end in their analysis to the conception of time, which is the measure of motion. Thus the definition is circular. And to this he referred by his saying: (So there occurs in its definition), i.e., the definition of gradualness, (time, and it is defined as the measure of motion, so circularity follows). Imam al-Razi said: Some virtuous people answered that the conception of the instantaneous in the clear aspect is clearer than the definiendum by this aspect? It ends, arising from lack of reflection. Since you know that the gist of the mentioned answer is what he mentioned by his saying: 'And it can be said, etc.' individuals and the state of accepting the second is nothing. His saying: (Because its occurrence, etc.) It is not meant what is apparent and understood from the expression, since the thing occurs twice while it is not gradual. Rather it is meant that nothing is connected in the interpretation of the absolute. His saying: (On the conception of time) When the mind assumes it divided after the two parts united with the whole in reality, and the name precedes the other with respect to time, and that thing occurs gradually, i.e., it cannot exist such that its supposed parts are assembled. And this applies to motion in the sense of traversal (qat') and what is in motion in the sense of mediation (tawassut): its gradual occurrence is its occurrence in an instant relative to the two limits of the distance after its occurrence in another instant. So its gradualness is with respect to the accidental relations, i.e., it cannot obtain at the limits of the distance, but with respect to its essence it is instantaneous. And with what we have clarified of the meaning of gradualness, the doubt that Imam al-Razi mentioned in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya is repelled, where he said: I have a doubt about gradualness, because gradualness does not occur without change, and change only occurs due to the occurrence of something or the disappearance of something from something instantaneous and occurring, and that the beginning of its existence obtains – if it does not obtain in its entirety, it obtains instantaneously because the beginning of events is instantaneous, and if it does not obtain in its entirety, then what remains is other than what occurred, due to the impossibility of one thing existing and being non-existent at the same moment. Thus there is no single thing that obtains gradually; rather there are successive things. So the result is that one thing in essence cannot have obtaining except instantaneously. Yes, a thing that has many parts can be said to have gradual obtaining in the sense of each [part].

His saying: (Circularity follows) It may be said: The gradualness occurring in the definition of time is the linguistic gradualness interpreted by the linguistic time more general than what Aristotle defined, so there is no problem. And you are aware that it is close to what Imam [al-Razi] mentioned.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the circularity in defining motion. It begins by presenting the definition of the First Teacher (Aristotle) and his followers: motion is an exit from potentiality to actuality gradually. They used expressions like 'little by little' or 'not instantaneously' or 'gradually'. Later philosophers changed this definition because 'gradualness' is defined as occurring in time after time, and analyzing the definition leads to time, which is itself defined as the measure of motion, resulting in circularity. The author explains that 'gradualness' depends on 'instant', which depends on 'time', and time is defined by motion. Then Imam al-Razi mentions an attempted answer by some scholars, which is criticized as insufficient. The author then elaborates on the meaning of 'gradualness' in motion, distinguishing between motion as traversal (qat') where parts are successive, and motion as mediation (tawassut) where gradualness is in the relations to limits, but the essence is instantaneous. Finally, a doubt from al-Razi is repelled: gradualness does not imply a single thing existing over time; rather, it is a succession of instantaneous occurrences. The passage ends with a possible resolution: the gradualness in the definition of time is linguistic, not technical, so circularity is avoided.

آني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition is acceptable because it uses familiar terms.
Correction: The terms themselves depend on the concept, leading to circularity.

Checkpoint
What is the circularity in the philosophers' definition of motion as gradual change?
A) It uses time, which is defined as the measure of motion.
B) It uses space, which is infinite.
C) It uses velocity, which requires motion.
D) It uses force, which is abstract.

Key Takeaway
Defining motion as gradual change leads to circularity because gradualness depends on time, and time is defined by motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The circularity here arises from defining motion by time and time by motion. In your own life, are there ideas you rely on that depend on each other, leaving no real foundation? What would it take to break that cycle?
?
This lesson shows even great philosophers can fall into logical error. How does this humble your own intellectual confidence? Will you scrutinize your most cherished beliefs for hidden circularities, starting today?

Repelling the Doubt about Gradualness in Motion
Addresses al-Razi's doubt on gradualness and the circularity issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doubt about gradualness raised by al-Razi and how it is resolved.

Core Idea
Gradualness in motion is a succession of instantaneous events.

Imam al-Razi raised a doubt: if motion is gradual, how can a single thing exist over time? The response clarifies that motion consists of successive parts, each occurring instantaneously. The passage also offers a final resolution to the circularity issue using linguistic gradualness.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's doubt
Read the part where al-Razi questions how gradualness can apply to a single thing.
Note his argument that change requires instantaneous events.
2
Understand the response
See how the author explains that gradualness is about successive parts, not a single enduring thing.
Pay attention to the distinction between traversal and mediation.
3
Analyze the resolution
Consider the final point that linguistic gradualness avoids circularity.
This resolves the earlier problem.

Commentary

Misconception: Gradualness requires a single thing changing over time.
Correction: Gradualness is about a series of instantaneous events, not a single enduring thing.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve al-Razi's doubt about gradualness?

The doubt about gradualness is resolved by understanding motion as a succession of instantaneous events, and the circularity can be avoided by using a linguistic definition of gradualness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of your spiritual growth as a slow, gradual transformation. But this lesson says that nothing is gradual in itself — only a succession of instantaneous choices. Consider this very moment: which single instant are you failing to seize for Allah's sake? What is holding you back from an instantaneous turn to Him right now?
?
Al-Razi doubted that a single thing can change gradually. Yet you treat your habits — prayer, patience, humility — as if they are one continuous thing you are 'working on.' Are you deluding yourself that you will fix it over time, instead of realizing that each instant is a separate creation from Allah? What instant have you wasted today that you cannot reclaim?
?
The author says gradualness is only in relation to limits, not in essence. Your life between birth and death is like motion between two limits. Are you living as if each instant is an independent gift from Allah, or are you coasting on past deeds and future hopes? What will you do in the next instant to make it count for eternity?

Definition of Motion as Tawassut (Intermediateness)
Defines motion as tawassut with two equivalent expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of motion as tawassut (intermediateness) and its two equivalent expressions as defined by Aristotle.

Core Idea
Motion is not a thing but a state of being always between the start and end.

This lesson introduces a fundamental concept in physics and metaphysics: the definition of motion as a state of intermediateness. Aristotle distinguished two meanings of 'motion', and here we explore the first: the body's continuous intermediateness between the starting point and endpoint. The passage also provides two equivalent ways to express this idea.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Aristotle uses a very precise style here.
2
Identify Aristotle's two meanings
The passage begins by stating that motion is said in two ways. Identify the two meanings mentioned. Focus on the first meaning, which is the subject of this passage.
The second meaning is not elaborated here.
3
Understand the definition of tawassut
The first meaning is described as a quality and description by which the body is always intermediate between the beginning and end of the distance. Note the key elements: orientation towards the goal, continuous intermediateness, and being in different spaces at each instant.
The body is never in the same space for two consecutive instants.
4
Explore the two equivalent expressions
The passage gives two expressions for the same definition. The first: the body is such that for any assumed limit, it is not present in that limit before or after the instant of arrival. The second: the body is such that at any assumed instant, its state differs from its state in the surrounding instants.
Both expressions capture the idea of continuous change without rest at any point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب أرسطو إلى أن الحركة تقل بالاشتراك اللفظي لمعنيين الأول التوجه إلى المقصد وهو كيفية وصفة بها يكون الجسم أبداً متوسطاً بين المبدأ والمنتهى اللذين للمسافة ولا يكون في حيز من الأحياز الواقعة فيما بين المبدأ والمنتهى آنين بل يكون في كل آن في حيز آخر ويسمي الحركة بمعنى التوسط، وقد يعبر عنها بأنها كون الجسم بحيث أي حد من حدود المسافة يفرض لا يكون هو قبل آن الوصول إليه ولا بعده حاصلا فيه وبأنها كون الجسم فيما بين المبدأ والمنتهى بحيث أي أن يفرض يكون حاله في ذلك الآن مخالفاً لحاله في آنين يحيطان به.
قوله : ( وصفة) أي المراد بالكيفية المعنى اللغوي إذ كونها كيفية اصطلاحية لم يثبت . قوله: (الذين) للمسافة تخصيص المسافة بالذكر لأن وقوع الحركة فيها يتفق عليه وتصويره فيها سهل فإن وجود المبدأ والمنتهى فيه والتوسط محقق . قوله: (لا) يكون قبل أن الوصول إليه لاخفاء في أنه لا يمكن الحصول في حد قبل آن
قوله : (وصفة) أشار بزيادتها إلى أنها المرادة بالكيفية فلا يلزم كون الحركة بمعنى التوسط من مقولة الكيف كما يوهمه عبارة المتن. قوله: (الذين للمسافة إشارة إلى دفع الاعتراض بأن المبدأ والمنتهى ليس إلا مبدأ الحركة ومنتهاها، فيكون تعريف الحركة بنفسها لكن فيه نظر لأن هذا إنما يفيد إن لم يتحقق الحركة بمعني التوسط إلا في الحركة الأينية، ويمكن أن يقال : المراد بالمسافة مطلق ما وقع فيه الحركة مجازاً والأقرب أن يقال بدل قوله للمسافة لما وقع فيه الحركة
Aristotle held that motion is said by verbal homonymy for two meanings: the first is the orientation towards the goal, which is a quality and a description by which the body is always intermediate between the beginning and the end that belong to the distance, and it is not in any of the spaces lying between the beginning and the end for two instants, but rather is in each instant in another space, and he calls it motion in the sense of intermediateness. And it is sometimes expressed as the body being such that whatever limit of the limits of the distance is assumed, it is not present in it before the instant of arrival nor after it; and as the body being between the beginning and the end such that whatever is assumed, its state at that instant is different from its state in the two instants surrounding it.
His saying: (and a description) means that what is intended by quality is the linguistic meaning, since it being a technical quality is not established. His saying: (that belong to the distance) specifies the distance because the occurrence of motion in it is agreed upon and its depiction in it is easy, for the existence of the beginning and the end in it and the intermediateness are realized. His saying: (not) being before the instant of arrival because it is not hidden that it cannot be obtained in a limit before the instant.
His saying: (and a description) indicated by its addition that it is what is intended by quality, so it does not entail that motion in the sense of intermediateness belongs to the category of quality, as the wording of the text suggests. His saying: (that belong to the distance) is an indication to repelling the objection that the beginning and the end are nothing but the beginning of the motion and its end, so the definition of motion would be by itself; but there is a consideration because this only benefits if motion in the sense of intermediateness is not realized except in local motion, and it can be said: what is meant by distance is, metaphorically, whatever motion occurs in, and the closer is to say instead of his saying 'for the distance' 'for what motion occurs in'.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines motion in the sense of tawassut (intermediateness). It begins by stating Aristotle's position that the word 'motion' is used homonymously for two meanings. The first meaning is the 'orientation towards the goal', which is a quality and description by which a moving body is always in a state of being intermediate between the starting point (mabda') and the endpoint (muntaha) of the distance (masafa). It specifies that the body is never in any one space for two consecutive instants; rather, it occupies a different space at each instant. This definition is then expressed in two equivalent ways: (1) the body is such that for any assumed limit along the distance, it is not present in that limit either before the instant of arrival or after it, and (2) the body is such that at any assumed instant, its state is different from its state in the two instants surrounding it. The commentator then explains key terms from the definition. First, he clarifies that the 'quality' here is not the technical Aristotelian category but a linguistic quality. Second, he notes that specifying 'the distance' is necessary because local motion is the paradigmatic case. Third, he addresses a potential objection: if the beginning and end are defined only as the start and end of the motion itself, the definition would be circular. The response is that the term 'distance' is meant metaphorically to include any medium in which motion occurs. The definition is thus secured without circularity.

Key Terms
الحركة بمعنى التوسط
المبدأ والمنتهى
المسافة
الكيفية
الاشتراك اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motion as tawassut is a simple quality.
Correction: The passage clarifies it is a relational description involving the body's position relative to the distance's limits, not a simple quality.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, what is the first meaning of motion according to Aristotle?
A) The body's transition from potentiality to actuality
B) The body's continuous intermediateness between the starting point and endpoint
C) The body's traversing of distance
D) The body's change of quality

Key Takeaway
Motion as tawassut describes a body's continuous intermediateness between the start and end of a distance, expressed in two equivalent formulations: the body is never at a limit before or after the instant of arrival, and its state at any instant differs from surrounding instants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your life is like motion as tawassut—never truly settled at any point—are you clinging to a moment of comfort, pretending you can stay when every instant you are moving toward an end? What would it mean to embrace that constant change and let go of false stability?
?
The body cannot be at the same space for two instants. Is your heart also never in the same state for two moments? In which direction is it moving—toward the Creator or away? How can you steer your constant change toward what pleases Allah?

Motion as an Intermediate State: Answering the Objection of Indivisible Parts
Objection and answer: motion as continuous vs discrete

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defines motion as a continuous intermediate state and how he replies to the objection that this would entail indivisible parts.

Core Idea
Motion is one continuous essence; only its successive limits are accidents.

A key debate in Islamic philosophy and kalam concerns the nature of motion. Is it a series of discrete states, or a continuous process? In this passage, the author defends the latter view by distinguishing between motion's continuous essence and the accidental attributes that accompany its passing through points. This distinction answers the atomistic objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector argues that if motion is attaining limits, it becomes a succession of instants, implying atoms. Notice the logical chain.
2
Identify the author's answer
He distinguishes between the motion itself (one continuous intermediate state) and the accidental attainments of limits. See how he uses the analogy of whiteness.
3
Examine the role of accidents
The author clarifies that the multiplicity is in the accidents (ʻawāriḍ), not the essence. This is the core of the reply.

For universals in the external world. Therefore, existent motion is the attainment of a specific limit, and that attainment is an instantaneous matter indivisible in the extension of distance, and what follows it is different from it. Thus motion is composed of successive instantaneous existent things, necessitating that distance is composed of indivisible parts, which is false according to them. We said: Motion in the sense of intermediate state (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-tawassuṭ) is an existent thing in the instant and continues through the continuation of time, in the sense that it exists in every instant supposed in that time, like a single whiteness existing in the instant while continuing in time. It is individuated by the unity of the subject, time, and what it is in. So numerically one motion is the intermediate state between the beginning and end attained by one subject in one time in one thing. If specific limits are supposed in the distance, then when the moving thing reaches one of them, that intermediate state acquires an accident of becoming an attainment in that midst and an arrival at that limit. This is an additional thing beyond its personal essence, an accident. When the body leaves the limit, an accident of its individuated essences has ceased and another accident occurs. Then these accidents succeed each other such that a third accident cannot be supposed between two successive ones of them, which is unimaginable except by the succession of points in the distance. If this is impossible, the succession of accidents is also impossible. (And it is in this sense) i.e. motion in the sense of orientation and intermediate state (opposes stability, i.e. the two limits.) That. His saying: (And what follows it etc.) i.e. the attainment that follows it is different from the first attainment, so there is difference. His saying: (successive) because without succession, the interruption of motion would be necessary. His saying: (For us, motion in the sense of intermediate state etc.) Its conclusion is that motion remaining from the beginning of the distance to the end is one in person, no multiplicity in its essence, only in its accidents. So the statement that attainment at a specific limit is other than attainment at another limit, if considered with respect to its essence, is incorrect; if considered with respect to its superadded accidents, they are successive. Thus the question is repelled. His saying: (in the sense etc.) Not in the sense that motion in the sense of cutting (al-qaṭʿ) applies to it. His saying: (like whiteness etc.) Means that it is instantaneous in existence, temporal in duration, like whiteness and other stable qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-qārra). It [the motion] is before the interruption of motion. So whether one says: the cause of motion there is the compeller (al-qāsir) or a power acquired from the compeller, as will be stated, or the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) with it, the concurrence of two complete causes upon a single effect is necessitated, either with respect to the intermediate state itself or with respect to the power acquired from the compeller. Let one reflect carefully until reflection. His saying: (And that attainment is an instantaneous matter indivisible) It was said: The instant (al-ān) according to the philosophers is not existent; rather it is an imaginary boundary shared between past and future. So how can it be applied to motion in the sense of intermediate state which is existent according to them? And it was answered that its non-existence does not preclude the application, just as the non-existence of motion in the sense of cutting does not preclude its application to distance which is existent according to them. So reflect.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against motion as an intermediate state (tawassuṭ) and the author's answer. The objector argues: if motion is the attainment of a specific limit, then each attainment is instantaneous and different from the next, so motion is composed of successive instantaneous parts, which would mean distance is composed of indivisible parts (atoms) — a view the philosophers reject. The author answers by defining motion as the continuous intermediate state between start and finish, which is one continuous entity individuated by subject, time, and object. The successive attainments of specific limits are not parts of the motion's essence but accidents (ʿawāriḍ) that occur to it. These accidents succeed each other, but the motion itself remains one. Thus the objection is repelled: motion does not require atoms; it is the accidents that are successive, not the essence. The glosses clarify that 'motion as intermediate' is like whiteness — instantaneous in existence but enduring in time — and that the philosophers' denial of the instant's existence does not prevent its applicability as a conceptual boundary.

Correction: The author distinguishes between the continuous essence of motion (one) and the discrete accidents that occur to it (multiple). The essence does not become composite.

Motion is one continuous intermediate state; its apparent division into limits is only accidental, not essential.

Motion as Intermediate Opposes Rest at Both Ends
The author explains that motion as intermediate is contrary to rest in both the starting and ending place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion as an intermediate state is contrary to rest in both the place of departure and the place of arrival.

Core Idea
Motion as intermediate opposes rest at both ends.

In this passage, the author refines the concept of motion by distinguishing it from simple rest at a destination. He argues that if motion is understood as an intermediate state between two points, it cannot be compatible with stability at either endpoint. This subtle point resolves a potential confusion about the opposition between motion and rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the original Arabic passage and the literal translation to get the full sense of the argument.
Notice the technical terms: istiqrār (stability), sukun (rest), and al-kawn fī al-ḥayyiz al-thānī (existence in the second place).
2
Identify the two types of opposition
The author states that motion as intermediate is contrary to rest in both the place being left and the place being entered. Understand the reasoning for each: for the first it's obvious; for the second it's because stabilization at the destination would mean the object is no longer intermediate.
The key insight is that an intermediate cannot be stable at the endpoint.
3
Contrast with the alternative definition
Compare motion as intermediate with the view that motion is simply existence in the second place. In that view, motion only opposes rest in the starting place, not the destination.
This contrast clarifies the unique nature of motion as continuous transition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما استقرار المتحرك في حيز واحد سواء كان منتقلاً عنه أو منتقلاً إليه أما منافاته للأول فظاهرة وأما منافاته للثاني فلأنه لو استقر بعد المبدأ في حيز لكان حاصلاً في المنتهى لا متوسطاً بينه وبين المبدأ ( فتكون ) الحركة ( ضداً للسكون في الحيز المنتقل عنه و للسكون في الحيز المنتقل ( إليه ) أيضاً ( بخلاف من جعلها ) أي الحركة ( الكون في الحيز الثاني ) فإنها إذا جعلت نفس الكون في الحيز الثاني كانت مضادة للسكون في المنتقل عنه دون السكون في المنتقل إليه كما مر
What is the stability (istiqrār) of the moving thing in a single place, whether it is the place being left or the place being entered. As for its opposition to the first, it is obvious; as for its opposition to the second, it is because if it stabilized after the beginning in a place, it would be an attainment in the end, not an intermediate between it and the beginning. (Thus) motion (is contrary to rest in the place being left) and (to rest in the place being entered) also. (Unlike the one who makes it), i.e. motion, (existence in the second place). For if it is made the very existence in the second place, it would be contrary to rest in the place being left but not to rest in the place being entered, as has passed.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage classifies motion as intermediate (tawassuṭ) in relation to rest. The author argues that motion as intermediate is contrary to rest in both the place the object leaves and the place it enters. The reason: if the object stabilized in a place after starting, it would have reached the end and would not be intermediate. Therefore, the intermediate state inherently opposes rest at both endpoints. In contrast, if one defines motion as 'existence in the second place' (al-kawn fī al-ḥayyiz al-thānī), it only opposes rest in the starting place, because the object is simply located at the destination, not in transit.

Key Takeaway

The author distinguishes between two concepts of motion—which one describes your current state, and what would need to change for you to be truly in transit?

6 min
The Basis of Motion as Intermediate: Continuity of Spaces and Rejection of Atomism
The author shows that motion as intermediate requires continuity of spaces and refutes atomism, with a nuanced conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of motion as intermediate (tawassuṭ) depends on the continuity of space and the rejection of the atom (the indivisible part).

Core Idea
Motion as intermediate requires continuous space, not atomic leaps.

This passage from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif delves into the foundational assumption behind the kalām concept of motion as intermediate (tawassuṭ). The author explains that if space were composed of indivisible atoms, motion would be a series of discrete jumps, not a continuous intermediate state. However, he also clarifies that even on the atomic hypothesis, one can still conceive a kind of tawassuṭ by treating multiple atoms as a distance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice the dense technical language and the inline glosses.
The passage includes commentary within parentheses.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Look for where the author contrasts a body composed of atoms (jawāhir afrād) with the rejection of the indivisible part. Understand how each scenario affects the nature of motion.
3
Understand the doubt and its refutation
Some thinkers doubted that motion as intermediate can occur in an instant. The author refutes this by explaining that movement involves a gradual departure from the starting limit.
This is a key philosophical move.
4
Pay attention to the final nuance
The author concludes that even if atoms exist, tawassuṭ can still be imagined by considering several atoms as a distance with a beginning and an end.
This prevents a too-simple rejection of atomism.
5
Summarize the main argument
Write a brief summary in your own words linking the basis of tawassuṭ to the continuity of spaces.
This helps consolidate understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن مبناه أي مبنى ذكره من الحركة بمعنى التوسط ووجودها في الخارج اتصال الأحياز في أنفسها وعدم تفاصلها إلى أمور لا تنقسم أصلا بناء على نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزئ وسنتكلم عليه ونستوفي القول فيه وذلك لأن الجسم إذا كان مركباً من الجواهر الأفراد، فإذا تحرك لم يكن هناك حركة واحدة ومتحرك واحد، بل هناك حركات ومتحركات بعد تلك الجواهر، فالمتحرك الواحد هو الجوهر الفرد الواحد، وإذا كانت المسافة مركبة من تلك الجواهر وفرضنا أن جوهراً واحداً انتقل من جوهر إلى جوهر آخر متصل به فقد حصل الحركة قطعاً، وليس هناك توسط بين المبدأ والمنتهى، بل ليس هناك إلا الكون في الجوهر الأول وليس بحركة قطعاً، والكون في الجوهر الثاني وهو الحركة المعرفة بالكون الأول في الحيز الثاني، وأما إذا قيل بامتناع الجوهر الفرد فالجسم إذا انتقل من مكان إلى آخر فلا بد أن يكون بينهما امتداد منه ، وتركب الجسم منقسم في جهة الحركة هو المسافة فالمكان الأول مبدأ تلك المسافة والمكان الثاني قوله : ( ومتحركات إلخ اعتبار تعدد المتحركات مما يتوقف عليه بيان المذكور ولذا لم يتعرض الجسم متصلا واحدا. قوله : (انتقل من جوهر إلخ يكون انتقاله دفعياً من غير توسط مسافة فهو بيان للواقع من أن انتفاء التوسط في صورة انتقال الجوهر الفرد أظهر. قوله : فلا بد أن يكون بينهما لأن انتقال الجسم من المكان الأول يكون بزوال انطباق طرفه على حد من المسافة والحدان لا بد أن يكون بينهما حاصل في المسافة فاندفع الشبهة التي أوردها بعض الناظرين على الحركة بمعنى التوسط وهي أنها تحدث في آن ففي ذلك الآن لا بد أن يكون الجسم في مكان فذلك المكان إما المكان الأول وأنه محال لأن المكان الأول محل السكون ، وإما المكان الثاني وأنه محال أيضاً، لأن المكان الثاني لا يحصل الجسم فيه قطعاً إلا قوله : (وليس هناك (توسط) بهذا التقرير يعلم أن المراد من بناء الحركة بمعنى التوسط على انتفاء الجزء أنه قد يتحقق الحركة على تقدير ثبوته ولا توسط أصلاً لا أنه لا يتحقق الحركة بمعنى التوسط أصلاً، إذ على تقدير ثبوت الجزء يتحقق الحركة بمعنى التوسط بأن يتصور عدة أجزاء مسافة ويعتبر لها مبدأ ومنتهى والجزء الفرد يتحرك من المبدأ إلى المنتهى .
And know that its basis – i.e. the basis of what was mentioned of motion in the sense of intermediate state (al-tawassuṭ) and its existence in the external world – is the continuity of spaces (ittiṣāl al-aḥyāz) in themselves and their not being divisible into things that are not divisible at all, based on the negation of the indivisible part (al-juzʾ alladhī lā yatajazzaʾ). We will discuss it and do justice to it. That is because if a body is composed of individual atoms (al-jawāhir al-afrād), then when it moves, there is not one motion and one moving thing; rather there are motions and moving things according to those atoms. So the one moving thing is the single individual atom. If the distance is composed of those atoms and we suppose that one atom moves from one atom to another adjacent to it, motion has certainly occurred, but there is no intermediate state (tawassuṭ) between the beginning and the end; rather there is only existence (kawn) in the first atom – which is certainly not motion – and existence in the second atom – which is the motion defined by the first existence in the second place. But if the impossibility of the individual atom is affirmed, then when a body moves from one place to another, there must necessarily be between them an extension from it, and the composition of the body divisible in the direction of motion is the distance. So the first place is the beginning of that distance and the second place. His saying: (and moving things etc.) The consideration of the multiplicity of moving things depends on the clarification of what was mentioned, and therefore he did not address the body as one continuous whole. His saying: (he moved from one atom etc.) Its being a sudden movement without an intervening distance is an explanation of the fact that the absence of intermediate state in the case of the movement of an individual atom is more apparent. His saying: (then there must be between them) because the movement of the body from the first place occurs by the cessation of the coincidence of its extremity with a limit of the distance, and between the two limits there must necessarily be something present in the distance. Thus the doubt raised by some thinkers on motion as intermediate state is repelled – namely that it occurs in an instant, and in that instant the body must be in a place: either the first place – impossible because the first place is a place of rest – or the second place – impossible also, because the body certainly does not attain the second place except... His saying: (and there is no intermediate state) by this exposition it is known that the intention behind building motion as intermediate on the negation of the part is that motion may be realized on the supposition of its existence without any intermediate state at all; it does not mean that motion as intermediate is not realized at all, because on the supposition of the existence of the part, motion as intermediate is realized by imagining several parts of distance and considering for them a beginning and an end, and the individual part moves from the beginning to the end.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage explains the foundational assumption behind the concept of motion as intermediate (tawassuṭ): the continuity of spaces (ittiṣāl al-aḥyāz) and the rejection of the indivisible part (atom). The author states that if bodies are composed of atoms, then motion is not a single continuous intermediate state but rather a series of instantaneous leaps between atoms. In that case, there is no tawassuṭ, only existence in the first atom (rest) and existence in the second atom (motion as being in the next place). Conversely, if atomism is false and space is continuous, then when a body moves, there is an extended distance, and the moving object passes through intermediate points. The glosses clarify a doubt: some object that motion as intermediate occurs in an instant, but the refutation is that the body's displacement involves a gradual departure from the starting limit. The final gloss explains that even if atoms exist, tawassuṭ can still be conceived by considering multiple atoms as a distance with a start and end, and the atom moves across them. Thus the basis is not that tawassuṭ requires continuity absolutely, but that its usual philosophical formulation relies on the continuity of space.

الجواهر الأفراد
الدفعية

Misconception: One might assume that rejecting atomism is necessary for motion as intermediate.
Correction: The author notes that tawassuṭ can still be imagined with atoms by considering multiple atoms as a distance.

Checkpoint

B) It is built on the continuity of spaces and rejection of the indivisible part.

D) It is only possible if motion is defined as being in the second place.

Key Takeaway
Motion as intermediate (tawassuṭ) is philosophically grounded in the continuity of space, but can still be conceptualized even on an atomic model by considering a series of atoms as a distance.

Motion as an Intermediate State Opposed to Rest
Defines motion as intermediate state opposing rest in both places

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that motion is defined as an intermediate state between the starting point and endpoint, and that it is opposed to rest in both the place left and the place entered.

Core Idea
Motion is the intermediate state, not just arrival at a new place.

This short passage clarifies a key definitional distinction in kalām: motion is not merely occupying a second location, but an intermediate state between two points. Understanding this opposition to rest in both places is essential before moving on to deeper discussions about motion's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then follow with the literal translation. Notice the key terms: الحركة (motion), السكون (rest), التوسط (intermediate), الحيز (place).
The passage uses parentheses in the original manuscript; they indicate editorial clarifications.
2
Understand the definition of rest
The passage defines rest (سكون) as the stable occupation of a single place. Identify the two types of places: the place moved from (منتقل عنه) and the place moved to (منتقل إليه).
غ is a typo for عنه. Rest can be in either place.
3
Grasp the two oppositions
Motion opposes rest in the place left because it is not settled there. It also opposes rest in the destination because if the moving object settled there, it would no longer be moving – it would have reached the endpoint, not be in between.
This is the key insight: motion is not just being in a new place; it is the process of being between places.
4
Contrast with a weaker view
The author contrasts his view with one that reduces motion to simply 'being in the second place'. That view can only explain opposition to rest in the first place, not the second. This shows why the intermediate-state definition is necessary.
This contrast reinforces the importance of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما استقرار المتحرك في حيز واحد سواء كان منتقلاً عنه أو منتقلاً إليه أما منافاته للأول فظاهرة وأما منافاته للثاني فلأنه لو استقر بعد المبدأ في حيز لكان حاصلاً في المنتهى لا متوسطاً بينه وبين المبدأ ( فتكون ) الحركة ( ضداً للسكون في الحيز المنتقل عنه و للسكون في الحيز المنتقل ( إليه ) أيضاً ( بخلاف من جعلها ) أي الحركة ( الكون في الحيز الثاني ) فإنها إذا جعلت نفس الكون في الحيز الثاني كانت مضادة للسكون في المنتقل عنه دون السكون في المنتقل إليه كما مر
The rest of the moving thing in one place, whether it is the place moved from or the place moved to. As for its opposition to the first, it is apparent. And as for its opposition to the second, it is because if it rests after the starting point in a place, it would be at the endpoint, not intermediate between it and the starting point. So motion is opposite to rest in the place moved from and also opposite to rest in the place moved to, unlike the view that makes motion the act of being in the second place. For if it is made the very act of being in the second place, it would be opposite to rest in the place moved from but not to rest in the place moved to, as has passed.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage defines motion as an intermediate state (tawassut) between the starting point and the endpoint, contrasting it with rest (sukun), which is confinement to a single place. The author explains that motion is opposed to rest in both the place left and the place entered. If motion were merely the act of being in the second place (al-kawn fi al-hayyiz al-thani), it would only be opposed to rest in the first place, not in the second. This definitional distinction sets the foundation for the subsequent discussion of motion's nature.

Correction: Motion is an intermediate state between two places, not merely occupancy of the second place.

Key Takeaway
Motion is defined as an intermediate state (tawassut) between two places, and it opposes rest in both the starting point and the destination.
