Motion as Intermediate State: The Atomist and Continuous Views
Explains how motion as intermediate state depends on continuity of spaces and denial of atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of motion as an intermediate state depends on rejecting indivisible atoms and how the two competing views of space (atomic vs. continuous) lead to different conceptions of motion.

Core Idea
Motion as an intermediate state is only possible if space is continuous, not atomic.

The definition of motion as an intermediate state (tawassut) between a starting point and an endpoint seems intuitive, but its philosophical foundation is not trivial. In this passage, the author examines two contrasting models of space: one where bodies are made of indivisible atoms, and another where space is continuous and infinitely divisible. He argues that only the continuous model allows for genuine intermediate states, which in turn grounds the reality of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow as the author presents two scenarios.
The passage uses technical terms like الجوهر الفرد (indivisible atom) and التوسط (intermediate state).
2
Identify the two scenarios
Look for the transition point: the first scenario assumes bodies are composed of atoms, the second denies the indivisible atom and assumes continuous space.
The transition is marked by the phrase 'وأما إذا قيل بامتناع الجوهر الفرد'.
3
Analyze the consequence of each scenario on motion
In the atomic scenario, motion is a discrete leap without any intermediate state. In the continuous scenario, motion involves a continuous intermediate state.
Notice how the author uses the concept of 'limits' and 'instants' to argue for continuity.
4
Reflect on why the denial of atomism is crucial
The passage establishes that the definition of motion as intermediate state is only coherent if space is continuous and infinitely divisible.
This connects to broader kalām debates about the nature of space and time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن مبناه ) أي مبنى ذكره من الحركة بمعنى التوسط ووجودها في الخارج ( اتصال الأحياز ) في أنفسها وعدم تفاصلها ) إلى أمور لا تنقسم ( أصلا بناء على نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزئ وسنتكلم عليه ونستوفي القول فيه وذلك لأن الجسم إذا كان مركباً من الجواهر الأفراد، فإذا تحرك لم يكن هناك حركة واحدة ومتحرك واحد، بل هناك حركات ومتحركات بعد تلك الجواهر، فالمتحرك الواحد هو الجوهر الفرد الواحد، وإذا كانت المسافة مركبة من تلك الجواهر وفرضنا أن جوهراً واحداً انتقل من جوهر إلى جوهر آخر متصل به فقد حصل الحركة قطعاً، وليس هناك توسط بين المبدأ والمنتهى، بل ليس هناك إلا الكون في الجوهر الأول وليس بحركة قطعاً، والكون في الجوهر الثاني وهو الحركة المعرفة بالكون الأول في الحيز الثاني. وأما إذا قيل بامتناع الجوهر الفرد فالجسم إذا انتقل من مكان إلى آخر فلا بد أن يكون بينهما امتداد منه، وتركب الجسم منقسم في جهة الحركة هو المسافة فالمكان الأول مبدأ تلك المسافة والمكان الثاني منتهاها، وتلك المسافة يمكن أن يفرض فيها حدود غير منقسمة في امتداد الحركة والمسافة نقطاً كانت أو خطوطاً أو سطوحاً، لا يمكن فرضها متتالية، وإلا كانت المسافة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ إما بالفعل أو بالقوة، وذلك محال فالمتحرك فيها له فيما بين مبدئها ومنتهاها حالة مخصوصة شخصية تختلف نسبتها إلى تلك الحدود الآنات المفروضة، التي لا يمكن أيضاً فرضها متتالية بل كل آنين مفروضين بينهما زمان يمكن أن يفرض فيه آنات أخر
And know that its foundation—i.e., the foundation of the mentioned definition of motion as intermediate state and its existence externally—is the continuity of spaces in themselves and their not being divided into indivisible things, based on the denial of the part that is indivisible, and we will discuss it and fully cover it. That is because if the body is composed of atomic individuals, then when it moves, there is not one motion and one moving thing, but rather motions and moving things corresponding to those atoms. Thus the single moving thing is the single atomic substance. And if the distance is composed of those atoms, and we suppose that one atom transfers from one atom to another adjacent to it, then motion certainly occurs, but there is no intermediate state between the start and the end. Rather, there is only the being in the first atom—which is certainly not motion—and the being in the second atom, which is the motion defined as being in the first place in the second place. But if it is said that the indivisible atom is impossible, then when the body transfers from one place to another, there must be an extension between them. And the composition of the body is divisible in the direction of motion, which is the distance. So the first place is the start of that distance, and the second place is its end. And in that distance, indivisible limits can be supposed along the extension of motion and distance—whether points, lines, or surfaces—but they cannot be supposed consecutive, otherwise the distance would be composed of indivisible parts either actually or potentially, and that is impossible. So the moving thing in it has, between its start and its end, a particular personal state whose relation to those supposed limits differs at the supposed instants, which also cannot be supposed consecutive; rather, between any two supposed instants there is a time in which other instants can be supposed.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage argues that the definition of motion as an intermediate state (tawassut) relies on the continuity of spaces (ittisal al-ahiyaz). The author examines two scenarios: (1) If bodies are composed of indivisible atoms (jawahir afrad), then motion is a series of discrete leaps—there is no true intermediate state, only being in one atom then another. (2) If atoms are rejected, then motion is continuous, and the distance is a continuous extension with infinitely divisible limits. The moving object then has a distinct state at each instant, which is the intermediate state. Thus, the denial of atomism is essential for motion to be understood as a continuous intermediate process.

الجوهر الفرد
المسافة

Correction: The author shows that the definition relies on denying the existence of indivisible atoms; if atoms exist, motion becomes a series of discrete leaps without any intermediate state.

Key Takeaway
The reality of motion as an intermediate state between endpoints depends on the continuity of space and the denial of indivisible atoms.

Motion as Traversal: A Mental Construct
Defines motion as traversal and argues it is mentally existent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second meaning of motion (traversal) is considered to have only mental existence and cannot be found in external reality.

Core Idea
Motion as traversal is a mental construct, not a real external entity.

In this passage, the author defines a second sense of motion: the complete traversal from start to end of a distance. He argues that this notion cannot exist outside the mind because at no moment is the entire traversal present. Let's examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the definition and the argument that follows.
The Arabic text may appear truncated, but the core argument is complete.
2
Identify the definition and the argument
Find where the author defines the second meaning of motion and where he gives the reason for its mental existence only.
3
Reflect on the metaphor
Consider the idea that when an object moves from point A to B, the motion as a whole is never present at any single moment. Why does that make it mental?
This connects to the broader kalām theme of distinguishing mental from external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعنى (الثاني) للحركة هو ( الأمر من أول المسافة إلى آخرها وهو الحركة بمعنى القطع ) ولا وجود لها إلا في التوهم ( لاستحالة وجودها في الأعيان ) إذ عند الحصول في الجزء الثاني من بطل نسبتها ( أي نسبة الحركة ) والأظهر أن يقال : بطل نسبته ( أي نسبة المتحرك ) إلى الجزء الأول منها ضرورة ) فلا يوجد هناك أمر ممتد من مبدئها إلى منتهاها، وبعبارة أخرى المتحرك ما لم يصل إلى المنتهى لم توجد الحركة بتمامها، وإذا وصل فقد
The second meaning of motion is 'the matter from the beginning of the distance to its end,' which is motion in the sense of traversal. It has no existence except in imagination, due to the impossibility of its existence in extramental reality. For when (the moving thing) is at the second part, its relation—i.e., the relation of motion (or better, the relation of the moving thing) to the first part—necessarily ceases. So there is no extended thing from its start to its end. In other words, as long as the moving thing has not reached the end, the motion in its entirety has not come to be, and when it has (reached) it has...
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage defines the second meaning of motion: the complete traversal (qat') from the beginning to the end of a distance. Unlike the first meaning (intermediate state), which exists externally, this meaning has no existence outside the mind. The reason is that when the moving object reaches the second part, the relation to the first part is lost, so there is no actual continuous entity spanning the entire distance. The motion as a whole is never present at any instant; it is only mentally constructed. The author emphasizes that until the object reaches the end, the motion is not complete, and once it reaches, the motion as traversal is past and not existent. Thus, it is merely a mental concept.

Key Terms

القطع

الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think that motion as traversal (e.g., the entire journey from A to B) is a real thing existing in the world.
Correction: The author argues that it has no external existence because it is never fully present at any moment; it is only mentally conceived as a whole.

D) Because it is defined as intermediate between places.

Key Takeaway
Motion as traversal has no external existence because it is never fully present at any moment; it exists only as a mental construct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your life journey, do you tend to envision it as a complete narrative from past to future — a 'traversal'? How does the lesson's assertion that such a traversal is only a mental construct challenge you to live more fully in the present moment, without clinging to an imaginary whole? What concrete action can you take today to stop treating your life as a mental storyline and instead engage with what is actually in front of you?
?
Consider a habit or spiritual goal you are trying to achieve (e.g., praying more consistently). Do you view it as a 'traversal' — a complete state you hope to reach? How might this mental construct be preventing you from actually making progress in the present? What would change if you focused only on the current moment and step, without the imagined end?
?
The author says motion as traversal is impossible in external reality because the relation to the start ceases. In your relationship with Allah, are you holding onto past spiritual states (e.g., a time of strong faith) as if they define your current journey? How can you release the need to see your life as a continuous story and instead submit to Allah in this very instant, without the mental construct of a 'whole'?

Motion as Qat' is Imaginary and Does Not Affect Time
Taftazani concludes that motion as qat' is imaginary and its divisibility does not undermine the argument for time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divisibility of motion in the sense of qat' does not prove the reality of time, because such divisibility is merely imaginary.

Core Idea
Imaginary divisions do not create real time.

In this concluding passage, Taftazani ties together the earlier discussion on motion and time. He argues that even though qat' motion can be divided in thought, this does not make it real. Therefore, the main argument for time's existence—based on the divisibility of motion—remains incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of Taftazani's conclusion.
Pay attention to the structure: the passage begins with a link to previous discussion, then states the main point, then gives two reasons.
2
Identify the two reasons (إما... وإما...)
Notice the 'إما... وإما' construction. The first reason is that the proof's reliance on divisibility is imaginary; the second is that time as measure of motion inherits motion's imaginary status.
These two reasons are alternative explanations for the incompleteness of the proof.
3
Understand 'imaginary' (amr wahmi)
An imaginary thing exists only in the mind, not externally. The divisibility of motion is only in imagination, so it does not prove real existence.
This is key to seeing why the argument fails.
4
Connect to earlier discussion
Taftazani references earlier findings that real motion and time are indivisible. The divided versions are mental pictures, so the proof for time is based on a mental construct.
Refer back to the section on time to see the contrast.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
After understanding the two reasons, articulate why the argument for time's existence is not conclusive according to Taftazani.
The passage ends by directing you to earlier material; consider how this affects the overall debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت تعلم من (هذا الذي ذكرناه في تحقيق الحركة معنى القطع وتصويرها أنّ قبولها للزيادة والنقصان والتقدم والانقسام لا يمنع أن يكون ) هي أمراً ( وهمياً ) لأنَّ قبولها لهذه الأمور، إنما هو بحسب التوهم، فإنَّ الأمر الممتد الموهوم يتصف بها قطعاً ( فلا يتم دليل إثبات الزمان ) وذلك إما لأنَّ العمدة في إثباته قبوله للزيادة والنقصان والتقدر والانقسام كما مر ويجوز أن يكون قبوله لها في التوهم فقط وذلك لا يمنع كونه أمراً وهمياً، وإما لأنَّ الزمان مقدار الحركة بمعنى القطع على المذهب المختار عندهم، فإذا لم يكن لهذه الحركة وجود لم يكن لمقدارها أيضاً وجود فيكون هذا معارضاً لأدلة وجوده ده فلا يترتب عليها مدلولها، وهو المراد بعدم تمامها وقد سلف منافي مباحث الزمان تحقيق أن الموجود من الحركة والزمان أمر لا ينقسم في امتداد المسافة وأنهما يرسمان في الخيال الحركة والزمان المنقسمين في ذلك الامتداد فارجع إليه.
And you know from this that we have mentioned in the investigation of motion in the sense of qat' and its depiction that its susceptibility to increase, decrease, priority, and divisibility does not prevent it from being an imaginary thing, because its susceptibility to these things is only in accordance with imagination, for the imagined extended thing is definitely characterized by them. Thus the proof for the existence of time is not complete. This is either because the mainstay in proving it is its susceptibility to increase, decrease, quantification, and divisibility, as mentioned, and it is possible that its susceptibility to them is only in imagination, and that does not prevent it from being imaginary; or because time is the measure of motion in the sense of qat' according to the preferred doctrine among them, so if this motion has no existence, then its measure also has no existence, so this would contradict the proofs for its existence, and thus they do not yield their implication. That is the meaning of its incompleteness. And it has already preceded in the discussions on time that the existent from motion and time is a thing that does not divide in the extension of distance, and that they depict in the imagination the divided motion and time in that extension, so refer back to it.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion and a note on the implications of the previous discussion. It states that motion as qat' is imaginary ('amrun wahmiyyun') because its attributes of divisibility etc. are only in the imagination. Therefore, the argument for the existence of time, which relies on the divisibility of motion, is not conclusive: either time's divisibility is also imaginary, or since time is the measure of motion and motion is imaginary, time too may be imaginary. The author references earlier discussions that the real existent of motion and time is indivisible, and the divided versions are mental representations.

Key Terms
أمر وهمي
الزيادة والنقصان
التقدم والانقسام
دليل إثبات الزمان
مقدار الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The divisibility of motion proves that time is real.
Correction: Divisibility is only in imagination, so it does not prove real time; moreover, time as a measure of imaginary motion is also imaginary.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof for time's existence fail according to Taftazani?
A) Because time does not exist at all.
B) Because motion's divisibility is imaginary, and time's divisibility may also be imaginary or time is the measure of imaginary motion.
C) Because the argument relies on sense perception which is unreliable.
D) Because time is a mental construct with no relation to motion.

Key Takeaway
The divisibility of motion is purely imaginary, so the argument for time based on divisibility is not conclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel that time is running out, are you reacting to a real objective thing or to an imaginary division you've created in your mind? What would change if you saw time as an imaginary measure rather than a real container?
?
Do you consider the moments of your life as real quantities that can be divided and measured, or do you recognize that your experience of time is a mental construction? How does this affect your sense of urgency or procrastination?
?
If the divisibility of time is only in imagination, what does that mean for your regrets about the past or anxiety about the future? Are you allowing imaginary divisions to control your present actions?

Motion in Categories: The View and Its Refutation Begun
Taftazani begins the third section on motion in categories and criticizes a specific definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a classical definition of motion occurring within a category and the initial objection against it using the example of blackness intensifying.

Core Idea
A category cannot be the real subject of its own motion.

Taftazani begins the third major discussion on motion by presenting a view held by some thinkers: that motion occurs in a category when that category itself changes gradually while remaining identical. This definition makes the category the real subject of motion, not the substance. Taftazani immediately rejects this as false, offering the example of blackening to show the logical difficulty. The passage is cut off, but the essential refutation is already grounded.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice that the text breaks off mid-sentence. Focus on the definition and the start of the refutation.
The source is incomplete; we only have the opening of the objection.
2
Identify the view being refuted
Find the definition of 'motion occurring in a category' given by 'a group.' It says the category itself changes gradually while remaining identical. This makes the category the real subject of motion.
Contrast this with the standard Ash'ari view that motion is an accident in a substance.
3
Examine the example of blackening
Taftazani uses the example of blackness (sawad) intensifying. If blackness itself is the subject of change, then either the original blackness ceases at intensification (so no change in it) or it remains unchanged (so no attribute occurs). This shows the view leads to absurdity.
The argument is cut off, but the core logical problem is clear: the category cannot act as its own subject.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
Since the text ends with 'because' (لأنَّ), we do not have the full conclusion. Consider what Taftazani would likely conclude: that motion cannot be in the category itself but rather in the substance.
This lesson is a fragment; later lessons may complete the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها ] ( فيما يقع فيه الحركة من المقولات عندهم ذهب جماعة إلى أن معنى وقوع الحركة في مقولة هو أن تلك المقولة مع بقائها بعينها تتغير من حال إلى حال على سبيل التدريج، فتكون تلك المقولة هي الموضوع الحقيقي لتلك الحركة، سواء قلنا : إِنَّ الجوهر الذي هو موضوع تلك المقولة موصوف بتلك الحركة بالعرض، وعلى سبيل التبع أو لم نقل وهو باطل لأن التسود مثلا ليس هو أن ذات السواد يشتد، ذلك السواد إن عدم عند الاشتداد، فليس فيه اشتداد قطعاً، وإن بقي ولم تحدث فيه صفة لأنَّ
[ The third objective: The occurrence of motion among the categories and its changes ] (Regarding what motion occurs in among the categories according to them: a group held that the meaning of motion occurring in a category is that that category, while remaining identical, changes from state to state gradually, so that category is the real subject of that motion, whether we say that the substance which is the subject of that category is described by that motion accidentally and derivatively, or we do not say. And it is false, because blackening, for example, is not that the essence of blackness intensifies—that blackness, if it ceases at intensification, then there is no intensification in it at all, and if it remains and no attribute occurs in it, because...
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage introduces the third objective (al-maqsad al-thalith) concerning the category in which motion occurs. A group of thinkers defined 'motion in a category' as the category itself changing gradually while remaining identical. According to this view, the category is the real subject of motion, and the substance is only an accidental subject. Taftazani declares this view false (batil) and provides the example of blackening: if blackness (sawad) is the essence undergoing intensification, then if the original blackness ceases when intensifying, there is no intensification; if it remains unchanged, no new attribute occurs. The argument is unfinished in the OCR.

Key Terms
الحركة في مقولة
الموضوع الحقيقي
التسود
السواد
الاشتداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion occurs in a category by the category itself changing gradually, making the category the real subject of motion.
Correction: Taftazani shows this is impossible: if the category changes, it either ceases or remains unchanged, neither of which allows genuine motion.

Checkpoint
What is the view that Taftazani refutes in this passage?
A) That motion is a category itself.
B) That motion occurs in a category by that category itself changing gradually while remaining the same.
C) That motion only occurs in substances.
D) That blackening is a type of motion in the category of quality.

Key Takeaway
A category cannot be the real subject of its own change because change would require it to either cease or remain unchanged, both of which are incoherent for motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a quality of your character—like your patience or anger—can intensify while remaining the same quality? According to this passage, what is the logical problem? How does this challenge the way you think about your own spiritual growth?
?
The argument shows that a category cannot be the subject of its own change. In what areas of your life do you claim that your identity remains unchanged while undergoing transformation? Is that coherent? What would change if you took this refutation seriously?
?
If a quality like blackness cannot change gradually without ceasing to be itself, what does that say about your attempts to change yourself? Are you trying to become a different person while holding onto the same old self? What must go and what must remain?
